Preface
This thesis has been prepared over five months at the Section for health technology, at
the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, and is written as the master thesis in Biomed­
ical Engineering. The project was nominated for 30 ECTS credits. The main supervisor
on the project was researcher Ying Gu. The project was made in collaboration with the
WARD project, where Jelle Reichert, Eske Kvanner Aasvang, and Christian Sylvest Mey­
hoff were external supervisors. The project was made by the students Benjamin Trasborg
(s184038) and Martin Riedel (s184032). The workload throughout the project has been
equally distributed between the students, and a table of contributions can be found in
appendix A.1. Figures without a reference are made by the authors.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of the human biology
and machine learning.

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Hypertension is one of the leading causes of death, worldwide, with around 1 billion peo­
ple affected. Hypertension can be a sign of many different diseases, and therefore it is
important to take action in time. Hypotension is of clinical interest, since it can be a sign
of different diseases. Both hyper­ and hypotension can result in major organ damage,
and therefore needs to be monitored to detect abnormalities as early as possible. The
monitoring of blood pressure (BP) is usually done using an uncomfortable inflatable cuff
with a low sampling rate. To avoid the inflatable cuff the BP can be found using machine
learning algorithms that predicts the BP using inputs from electrocardiograph (ECG) or
photoplethysmography (PPG) devices. Previous projects have used machine learning
(ML) algorithms to detect the BP using either the pulse transit time (PTT) or raw ECG and
PPG signal. However, using PTT requires synchronized sampling from the equipment
used to obtain the data, and not all monitoring equipment delivers perfect raw signals.
That is why an investigation of BP estimation based on vital signs and short ECG and
PPG segments has been investigated. This project focuses on classifying three systolic
blood pressure (SBP) states, HIGH, NORMAL and LOW, based on vital signs and raw
ECG and PPG signal segments from hospitalized 24/7 monitored patients. The classifi­
cation of SBP states was made by training two random forests on the vital signs and raw
segment signals. The vital signs used were heart rate (HR), pulse rate (PR), respiration
rate (RR) and peripheral oxygen saturation (SpO2). Features from both the vital signs
and the raw signals were calculated and used as inputs for the model. Calibration fea­
tures from the first three hours of the monitoring period were used in the model. The data
used is from the WARD project conducted at different Danish hospitals with 875 patients
suffering from different conditions. Linear interpolation of the vital sign measurements
was tested but did not improve the performance of the model. The data from the MIMIC
III database was tested, but since it was crucial that the data was obtained at the same
time, this data was not suitable for this project.
The final model was a combination of two random forests, one good at detecting the LOW
SBP states, and one good at detecting the HIGH SBP states. Post processing was added
to the final model to increase the accuracy of the model.
The final model ended up having an overall accuracy of 74.5 % on the test data. The
finding indicates that additional work must focus on further improving the model and the
quality and amount of data.

1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation and Problem statement
Hypertension, also known as increased BP, is one of the leading causes of death world­
wide. It can lead to extensive cardiovascular damage and organ damage, and approx­
imately 1 billion people worldwide are affected of hypertension. Hypertension can be
caused by many things, such as unhealthy lifestyle, low physical activity or abnormalities
in the cardio vascular system. Hypotension, which is a condition with decreased BP, can
be caused by blood loss or heart failure, and therefore needs medical treatment. Both
hypo­ and hypertension are of clinical interest, therefore it is important to measure the BP
of hospitalized patients, to give the patients the right treatment in time [1].
The BP is most commonly measured using an inflatable cuff. The cuff is placed around
the upper left arm and is then inflated to block the blood flow for a short time, while the
measurement is made. One single BP measurement is not a problem, but when patients
are continuously monitored for several hours or days there are some issues with the in­
flatable cuff. Using the cuff often causes underestimation of systolic BP, false readings
caused by improper cuff size, patient discomfort and low sampling frequency. With the
patient monitoring system ”Wireless Assessment of Respiratory and circulatory Distress”
(WARD), the cuff will inflate every 30 minute during day time, and during night time it will
happen once every hour.
Former studies have shown that it is possible to estimate the BP using ML algorithms,
see section 3. The ML models estimate the BP using input signals from different sensors
around the body.
Besides a BP monitor, the WARD setup has an ECG device and a photoplethysmography
(PPG) device attached to the patients, and these signals could be used as inputs for an ML
model. Estimating BP using an ML method in the WARD setup would solve the problems
with the discomfort and improper cuff size, and increase the frequency of BP estimations.
1.2
Aim of project
The aim of this project is to eliminate the use of the inflatable cuff in the WARD setup
and get the information about the SBP from the ECG and PPG devices. This problem is
attempted to be solved by classifying three intervals of SBP: LOW, NORMAL and HIGH.
From these levels alerts are given to the nurses and doctors when the patients’ SBP level
gets critical. The SBP level should be classified using a machine learning algorithm.
1.3
Requirements to project
Below is a list with the requirements related to the project
• The SBP intervals should follow the values given in table 1.1
• The model must be validated and tested on WARD data
• The model features must be possible to calculate using the features from the WARD
data.
2

a hypertension alerts there must be either 2 following SBP measurements above 180
mmHg or 1 single measurement above 220.

2
Background
2.1
WARD project
Figure 2.1: Patient connected to WARD ECG and SpO2 device [2] and doctor holding
gateway device
WARD is a Danish company founded in 2016, that has created a clinical wireless monitor­
ing support system for vital signs using artificial intelligence (AI) algorithms in real­time.
The AI­interpretation processes inputs from medical devices applied to the patient and
based on that the system is able to give alarms to the doctors and nurses when vital signs
from the patients get critical. The WARD system’s intended use is in patient settings
where continuous monitoring of patients can be an advantage. The WARD system has
been used for monitoring of postoperative high­risk patients, patients hospitalized with
chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), and patients with COVID­19 [2]. The
WARD system has a body­sensor network consisting of three devices, the ECG device
ISANSYS lifetouch (Isansys Lifecare, Oxfordshire), the PPG device Nonin WristOx 3150
(Nonin Medical inc., Minnesota, USA), and the BP device Meditech BlueBP­05 (Med­
itech Ltd., Hungary). Data from all 3 devices are transmitted through Bluetooth to a be­
side gateway with a secured hospital WiFi connection [3]. A patient connected to the ECG
and PPG device can be seen in figure 2.1. Since all the devices are wireless they are
powered by batteries. Transmitting data is a power consuming process, and therefore the
devices are only transmitting 10 continuous seconds of data every minute to extend the
lifetime of the batteries.
4
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:13==
2.1.1
WARD Devices
Figure 2.2: Data from WARD devices to gateway. Orange and blue signal are called
”Segment data” and black values are ”Vital sign data”. From the ECG device one RR
sample and one HR sample together with 10 seconds raw ECG segment is collected every
minute. From the PPG device one pulse rate sample and one SpO2 sample together with
10 seconds raw PPG segment is collected every minute. From the BP cuff one sample is
collected every 30 minute in the day and every 60 minutes in the night.
Figure 2.2 shows how data from the WARD devices are sent to the gateway.
The ECG device used in the WARD setup is the ISANSYS lifetouch (Isansys Lifecare, Ox­
fordshire), which is a wireless single­lead electrocardiogram chest patch that is able to in­
terpret the raw ECG signal and extract the average HR/minute and an average RR/minute.
The raw ECG signal is sampled at a frequency of 75 Hz but to save battery only 10 sec­
onds raw data from each minute is sent to the gateway [4].
In the WARD setup a wrist­worn pulse oximeter Nonin WristOx 3150 (Nonin Medi­ cal
inc., Minnesota, USA), measures the SpO2 in the finger tip.
The oximeter interprets
the raw PPG signal and extracts the average pulse rate (PR)/min and an average SpO2
(SpO2)/min. The raw PPG signal is also sampled at a frequency of 75 Hz, also sending
only 10 seconds of data every minute to save battery [4].
The Meditech BlueBP­05 (Med­ itech Ltd., Hungary) is a compact, wireless device for
intermittent non­invasive oscillometric measurements of BP. The device is programmed
to start BP measurements every 30 min during daytime and every 60 min during night­
time in the WARD setup. The device sends one BP measurement every 30 or 60 minute
[3].
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs

==PAGE:14==
2.2
Electrocardiogram
Figure 2.3: ECG and activation of the heart.[5]
To record and evaluate the electrical activity generated by action potentials of the heart,
an ECG is used. To perform an ECG, electrodes are placed around the chest to monitor
the signals. The electrical signals detected by the electrodes are then acquired by a
machine that shows the graph of the heart activity. A standard ECG signal is shown in
figure 2.3. The p­wave corresponds to the depolarization of the atrium, the QRS­complex
corresponds depolarization of the ventricles, and the T­wave represents the repolarization
of the ventricles. The shapes of the waves and duration between waves can then be used
to detect coronary heart diseases or other complications with the heart.
2.2.1
Heart rate
One of the most important features that can be extracted from the ECG is the HR. The
HR is the number of contractions the heart does every minute. HR is measured in beats
per minute (BPM). The HR of a healthy resting person is approximately 72 BPM [6].
2.2.2
Pan­Tompkins
Pan­Tompkins is a signal processing method often used when analyzing ECG signals.
The algorithm applies a series of different filters and is used for QRS detection. The
series of filters is used to cancel out the noise in the signal and highlight the frequency
content from the heart depolarization and repolarization. The method consists of the steps
shown in figure 2.4.
6
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:15==
Figure 2.4: Pan­Tompkins steps
Figure 2.5: Pan­Tompkins steps plotted for a WARD patient
Band­pass filter
The band­pass filter is used to remove noise from the ECG signal and thereby increase
the signal­to­noise ratio. Examples of noise that could be removed, would be muscle
signals if the patient is moving, or interference with the 50 Hz signal from the power grid
used in electronic devices in the room. The band­pass filter consists of a low­pass filtration
followed by a high­pass filtration. The low­pass filter removes all the high­frequency noise
and the high­pass filter removes all the low­frequency noise [7]. A band pass filtered signal
can be seen in figure 2.5 at the plot in the middle to the left.
Derivative filter
The derivative filter is used to amplify rapid changes, such as peaks in the signal. Which
will make the QRS detection easier [7]. A derivative filtered signal can be seen in figure
2.5 at the plot in the middle to the right.
Squaring
The squaring is used to make the signal positive to make it easier to combine all the peaks
from each QRS­complex in one spike. Besides making the signal positive, it also makes
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
7
==PAGE:16==
a non linear amplification by emphasizing the high frequency components, which makes
it easier to distinguish it from the rest of the signal [7]. A squaring filtered signal can be
seen in figure 2.5 at the plot in the bottom to the left.
Moving­window integration
Moving­window integration is used to combine all the peaks from each QRS­complex in
one spike. The filter also removes noise and smooth out the signal, which reduces the
chances of making false detections [7]. A moving­window integration filtered signal can
be seen in figure 2.5 at the plot in the bottom to the right.
Adaptive thresholding
Adaptive threshold is used to find the peak of each spike corresponding to a R­peak. An
adaptive threshold is a threshold that is calculated just for a smaller region of the signal,
which means the threshold will be different for each QRS­complex. Using an adaptive
threshold instead of a fixed threshold allows noise and changes in the signal while it is
still possible to detect the peaks [7]. The peaks found using adaptive thressholding can
be seen in figure 2.5 at the plot in the bottom to the right as the pink circles.
2.3
Photoplethysmogram (PPG)
Photoplethysmogram (PPG) is an optical technique that is used to detect changes within
oxygen in the peripheral blood. Oxygen is a crucial resource for living tissues, and there­
fore the saturation of oxygen in the blood is of clinical interest. To investigate the peripheral
capillary oxygen saturation (SpO2), an oximeter can be used. An oximeter has two dif­
ferent light sources operating at different wavelengths, one wavelength which is highly
absorbed in deoxygenated hemoglobin and one wavelength which is highly absorbed in
oxygenated hemoglobin. This is shown in figure 2.6. The light travels through the tis­
sue, often a finger or the ear lobe, before it arrives at a photodetector on the other side.
The SpO2 level is given as a percentage, and can then be calculated using equation 2.1,
where it is defined as the proportion of oxygen­saturated hemoglobin (HbO2) relative to
the overall quantity of hemoglobin in the blood. The overall quantity of hemoglobin in the
blood is the sum of oxygenated hemoglobin (HbO2) and deoxygenated hemoglobin (Hb)
[8].
SpO2[%] =
HbO2
HbO2 + Hb · 100
(2.1)
Figure 2.6: How SpO2 is measured using red and infrared light to detect the amount of
oxygenated and non­oxygenated hemoglobin [9].
8
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:17==
2.4
Blood pressure
Figure 2.7: BP decrease as the blood is pumped through the body, why the BP is highest
in the blood vessels right after the blood leaves the heart [10].
BP is a measure of the force that the blood applies on the blood vessels. In arteries,
the BP depends on the rhythmic contraction of the heart. Systolic pressure is when the
ventricles contract and pumps blood from the heart into the arteries, this is when the BP
reaches its maximum value. Diastolic pressure is when the ventricles relax and the BP
reaches its minimum value. The BP decreases as the blood moves through the blood
vessels resulting in a BP around 0 when the blood again reaches the right atrium. This
can be seen in figure 2.7. The standard unit for BP is millimeters of mercury (mmHg) [6].
2.4.1
Regulation of BP in the healthy body
The body has different ways to regulate the BP. The three major mechanisms are barore­
ceptors, chemoreceptor reflexes and the nervous system.
Baroreceptors
The baroreceptors are sensitive to stretch. They are located in the carotid sinuses and
the aortic arch. The baroreceptors can control the peripheral resistance, HR, and stroke
volume if the BP changes.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
9
==PAGE:18==
Figure 2.8: Barorecptor response to change in BP [11]
Figure 2.8 shows how the baroreceptors control the BP. If the BP gets too high the barore­
ceptors will register the stretch of the blood vessel wall and will then sent a signal to the
cardio­inhibitory center in the brain. This will decrease the sympathetic impulses to the
heart causing the HR to decline. The baroreceptors will also inhibit the vasomotor center
causing the vasomotor impulses to decline causing the blood vessel diameter to increase.
The combination of decreased HR and increased diameter of the blood vessels will result
in a decreased BP.
If the BP decreases the baroreceptors will register a decreased stretch of the blood vessel
wall, it will cause a stimulation of the vasomotor center in the brain. Then the blood vessel
will constrict, resulting in a decreased diameter in the blood vessels. The baroreceptors
will also stimulate the cardio acceleratory center in the brain which will increase the HR.
The constricted blood vessels and increased HR will increase the BP [6].
Chemoreceptor reflexes
Chemoreceptors are specialized cells which primary function is to measure and react to
the chemical composition in the blood. In the regulation of O2 and CO2, chemoreceptors
play a key role. If the chemoreceptors detect changes in the composition, a signal is sent
to the medulla oblongata which can regulate the RR and the depth of every breath. An
increment in the RR will increase the blood oxygen saturation and decrease the blood
CO2 saturation [6].
Hormonal mechanisms
Adrenal Medulla Mechanism
Increased sympathetic stimulation of the adrenal medulla causes release of epinephrine
and norepinephrine. Epinephrine increases the HR and the stroke volume and causes
10
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:19==
vasoconstriction. This causes an increase in the BP. Other hormonal mechanisms in­
clude the Renin­Angiotensin­Aldosterone Mechanism, Antidiuretic Hormone, and atrial
natriuretic mechanism but these mechanisms only affect the BP, blood vessel constric­
tion, and blood volume why they are not relevant regarding the vital signs.

Table 2.1: Table showing how vital signs affect each other to maintain homeostasis. The
green cell indicates which vital sign was changed initially, and the other cells then show
how the other vital signs will react [6].
From table 2.1 it is evident that there is a physiological connection between the five vital
signs, and they all are either direct or indirect linked together. This connection is essential
for making an ML algorithm to predict one of the vital signs. Without a physiological con­
nection, the ML algorithm would not be able to detect a connection which is not present.
2.4.2
Factors that can affect the BP
Many factors can affect BP such as physical activity, emotions, age, sex, BMI, and different
kinds of medication.
Physical activity
Having an active lifestyle can lower BP by keeping the heart and blood vessels in good
shape. During physical activity, the BP will increase to deliver more oxygen to the cells,
but under rest, the BP will be lower compared to not having any physical activity [12].
Age
With increased age follows thickening and reduced elasticity of the arterial walls caused by
atherosclerosis. Because of the thickening, the diameter is decreased, which is leading to
hypertension. This is why older people often will have a higher BP compared to younger
people [6].
Sex
The average SBP for women is lower than for men during early adulthood but around
the 50 years age it will be the other way around where women have higher SBP than
men. DBP is lower for women than for men regardless of age. The mechanisms that are
responsible for the sex difference in BP are not fully understood but sex hormones are a
part of the reason [13].
BMI
It has been proven that there is a strong correlation between BMI and BP. A high BMI
is often a sign of a high amount of fatty tissue in the body. Since the fatty tissue needs
a blood supply, the total blood volume is often increased for people with high BMI. In
general, a high BMI is also a sign of a larger body, and a larger body means the heart
has to pump more to supply the body with oxygen and nutrients. Furthermore, a high BMI
can be an indication of obesity. A side effect of obesity is insulin resistance, which is a
condition that triggers vasoconstriction [14].
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
11
==PAGE:20==
Medication
Many different kinds of medication have side effect that affects BP. Non­steroidal Anti­
Inflammatory drugs (NSAID) like Ibuprofen and Naproxen can make the body retain fluid
and thereby increase BP because of increased blood volume. Cough and cold medication
contain decongestants that can increase the HR and thereby the BP. Migraine headache
medication often works by constricting blood vessels in the head but it also constricts the
blood vessel in the rest of the body which can increase the BP. There are also different
kind of medication that is used to control BP like ACE inhibitor, angiotensin­2 receptor
blocker, calcium channel blocker, diuretics, and beta blockers [15]
How to measure BP
Figure 2.9: Measurement of BP [16]. Step 1. the cuff is inflated and blocks the artery. Step
2. the cuff is deflated until the first blood flows through the artery and the first Korothoff
sound can be heard. Step 3. The cuff is deflated until blood is flowing without turbulence
and no sound can be heard.
Figure 2.9 shows how an oscillometric BP device works. The rubber cuff is placed on the
left arm where it can block the brachial artery. A stethoscope is placed over the brachial
artery from where it can listen to the blood flow. First, the cuff is inflated until it blocks
the artery completely. This causes complete silence in the stethoscope since no blood
flows through the artery. Then the pressure in the cuff is lowered gradually until a sound
appears in the stethoscope. The first sound that will appear is used to determine the
SBP. The sound is made when the left ventricle contract and a small amount of blood
flows through the constricted artery with turbulent flow. Then the pressure of the cuff
is once again lowered until there is no sound in the stethoscope. This pressure is the
diastolic pressure where the pressure from the cuff is lower than the DBP causing the
12
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:21==
artery to remain open leading to a nonturbulent flow [6].
2.4.3
Hypertension
Hypertension is a common problem that affects approximately 1 in 4 of the adult popula­
tion. Hypertension is defined as SBP above 140 mmHg or DBP above 90 mmHg. The BP
is determined by the minute volume of the heart and the total peripheral resistance. Often
the cause of hypertension is an increment in the peripheral resistance, and to maintain
sufficient pressure to the vital organs, higher HR is required. Hypertension is often asso­
ciated with several lifestyle habits, such as smoking, stress, unhealthy diet, low physical
activity, and alcohol consumption. Furthermore, heredity has a large impact. The symp­
toms of hypertension are headaches, dizziness, blurred vision, chest pain, shortness of
breath, or nose bleeding but most of the time there are no symptoms. Due to the increased
pressure on the inside of the vessels, there is an increased risk of cardiovascular diseases
like apoplexy, acute myocardial infarction, heart failure, organ damage, or sudden death.
The best treatment for hypertension is a healthy lifestyle and weight loss. In some cases,
ACE inhibitors, angiotensin­2 receptor blocker, calcium channel blocker, and diuretics are
used to decrease the BP [1].
2.4.4
Hypotension
Hypotension is a condition where the SBP is lower than 90 mmHg, and the DBP is lower
than 60 mmHg. The condition is less common, compared to hypertension with around 10­
20% of the adult population. Many people with low BP experience few or no symptoms,
while other people can experience dizziness, pale skin, or being lightheaded. Hypotension
can be treated by increasing fluid and salt intake and in some cases, medications can be
used. Compression stockings can be used to increase the pressure on the legs and
thereby the veins, which increases the BP a bit [1].

2.6
Machine learning
2.6.1
Decision tree
A decision tree is a type of supervised learning algorithm that splits data into subsets
based on the value of one or more of the features. The subsets of data are passed
through the decisions, also called decision nodes. In a decision node, an evaluation of
the subset takes place. The subsets pass through nodes until the stop criteria is reached,
and a final prediction can be made [17]. Figure 2.10 shows an example of how a decision
tree can be designed using features from this project. An initial decision is made in the
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
13
==PAGE:22==
decision node, also called the root node. From here the data can be passed into 2 sub­
trees, which again consist of decision nodes. In the decision nodes, the data is evaluated
against a requirement. Depending on the decision the data can either go to a leaf node
or into another decision node. A leaf node is the final output node. If the data reaches a
leaf node the tree can not segregate further [18].
Figure 2.10: Decision tree example with features used in this project. The root node is
if the mean HR is above 100 BPM. Decision node 1 is if the maximum RR is above 20
breaths per minute. If the RR is above 20 BrPM the SBP is classified as HIGH and if it
is equal to or below it will be classified as NORMAL. Leaf node 2 decides if the minimum
SpO2 value is below 88 %. If the SpO2 is below 88 % the SBP will be classified as LOW.
If it is equal to or above 88 % it will continue to the third decision node that decides if the
STD of the PR is above 100 BPM. If the STD of PR is above 100 BPM the SBP will be
classified as HIGH and if it is equal to or below the SBP will be classified as NORMAL.
14
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:23==
Figure 2.11: Example of how the splits are calculated in the decision tree
Figure 2.11 shows how each split is calculated. At the top of the figure a table containing

three observation with two features and one SBP state is shown. To calculate how the
split should be in the root node each feature is sorted and the mean between each row
is found (seen in the green squares). Then the Gini impurity for each possible split is
calculated using equation 2.2. The information gain for each split is calculated using
equaiton 2.3 and the split with the largest information gain is used as the root node. In
this case it is the split shown in the red square. Since it is a clean split where each split only
contains one classification parameter this tree will only consist of one split. The decision
tree algorithm uses recursive binary splits, which means the dataset will be divided into
subsets or subgroups based on binary decisions (True/False).
The Gini impurity measures the impurity of a node in a decision tree. The Gini impurity is
calculated using following formula:

2.6.2
Random Forest classifier
A Random Forest classifier, also known as a bagged tree, is an ensemble learning al­
gorithm that is a combination of several decision trees. The multiple decision trees is to
improve the accuracy of the model. The random forest learning algorithm uses bagging
which stands for Bootstrap Aggregating, and is a technique used in machine learning to
improve the accuracy and stability of a machine learning model [20].
16
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:25==
Figure 2.12: Example of how the random forest is trained with the features from this
project. In the top a table with training data from one patient with five observations at
five different timestamps are shown. From each observations three different features
have been calculated, mean HR, mean SpO2, and mean RR. Bootstrapping is performed
making three new subsets that have been randomly selected using replacement. From
each subset a decision tree is trained using only some of the features. Each tree makes
a prediction and the majority vote will be the final prediction of the random forest.
When data is loaded into the random forest classifier, the training data is split into subsets.
Each subset, is used to train a decision tree. Every decision tree gives a prediction, and
the most frequently predicted class will be the final prediction of the model. In a random
forest, the bagging technique is used to create multiple decision trees, each of which is
trained on a random subset of the data. By combining the results of these trees, the
random forest produces a more robust and accurate model than a single decision tree.
Figure 2.12 shows how a random forest classifier works.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
17
==PAGE:26==
Matlab’s bagged tree algorithm is based on the CART (Classification And Regression
Trees) algorithm and includes the following steps:
1. Bootstrap Sampling
• For each tree in the ensemble, one bootstrap sample Db of size N is created
using random selection with replacement from the training set.
2. Decision Tree Training
• Each tree is trained independently on a bootstrap sample using the Gini impu­
rity and the information gain explain in section 2.6.1
3. Ensemble Prediction
• When the bagged tree ensemble is trained, the predictions of multiple trees are
combined to make a final prediction.
• The prediction is made using a voting mechanism where each tree casts a
vote on one of the classes. The class with the most votes will end up as the
classification.
2.6.3
Hyper parameters
Hyper parameters are parameters that are used to control the learning process of the
machine learning model. The hyper parameters are adjusted prior training of the model
and are optimized based on the training and validation set. When training a random forest
it is possible to adjust the hyper parameters, number of trees, maximum number of splits
and misclassification cost [21].
Number of trees
The number of trees needed in a random forest depends on the data, however a rule of
thumb says between 50 and 400 trees. Generally speaking, increasing the number of
trees in the random forest would improve the performance. However, having too many
trees can be a problem due to over­fitting, and therefore the number of trees is a hyper
parameter that needs to be tuned specific to the data [22].
Number of splits
The number of splits in a random forest corresponds to number of branches in each de­
cision tree. Each split creates new branches and further divides the data into subgroups
with smaller number of features. Having many splits can lead to a high level of detailed
detection in the decision making, however, having too many splits can lead to over­fitting.
Having too few splits can lead to under­fitting where the model is not able to predict the
outcome [23].
Misclassification cost
Misclassification cost is a way to specify the importance of predictions in a ML model. The
cost should be increased in the type of predictions that is most undesirable, for instance
on the false negative predictions. The cost then works as a weighted penalty for the ML
model to make a prediction that could result in a false negative prediction [24].
2.6.4
Evaluation metrics in machine learning
Training set
The training set is the part of the data that is used to train the model. This is typically the
largest part of the data, and in this project, the training set is 80% of the patients. From the
training data features are extracted and the model parameters are optimized to minimize
the error between what was predicted and the true value [25].
18
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:27==
2.6.5
Validation set
The validation set is a smaller part of the data set, and in this project the validation set
is 10% of the patients. The validation set is used to test the model during hyper param­
eter tuning. After each training the validation set is used on the model to check if the
performance has increased or decreased [25].
2.6.6
Test set
The test set is also 10% of the patients, which first will be introduced when the hyper­
parameters have been tuned. It will be used to test the model performance, just like the
validation set. The difference between the validation set and the test set is that the val­
idation set is used to optimize the model, while the test set only will be used when the
model is tuned. The reason for this is that the model must be tested on unseen data, to
avoid any kind of overfitting to the validation set [25].
2.6.7
K­fold cross validation
Figure 2.13: 5­fold cross validation [26]
K­fold cross­validation is a validation method used to test how well a model can predict
unseen data. The training data is split into k blocks. Then k iterations are trained on k­1
blocks as training data and the last block is used as the test set. The average error of the
k iterations is used to evaluate the model. An example of a 5­fold cross­validation can be
seen in figure 2.13 [26].
2.6.8
Confusion matrix
To evaluate the performance of a ML model on a classification problem, a confusion matrix
is often used. A confusion matrix can indicate how many of the observations in the test set
that was correctly classified, and how many were wrongly classified. Figure 2.14 shows
a confusion matrix based on the 3 detection classes in this project. ”True class” is the
class the observation belongs to in the data set, while the ”Predicted class” is which class
the ML model assigned the observation to be. The more observations in the diagonal in
the confusion matrix means more correctly classified observations and therefore a better
model.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
19
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.14: Confusion matrix with the 3 SBP state classes LOW, NORMAL, and HIGH
An observation can either be true positive, true negative, false positive, and false negative.
It is different from class to class if a field in the confusion matrix belongs to a true positive,
true negative, false positive, or false negative classified value [27]. The Different values
can be seen in figure 2.15
2.6.9
True Positive
A true positive prediction is when the model prediction is correctly classified [27]. The
true positive predictions are placed in the diagonal from upper left corner to the bottom
right corner in the confusion matrices seen in figure 2.14 and 2.15. Example from the top
confusion matrix in figure 2.15. If all of the true HIGH observations were predicted to be
in the HIGH class, the model would be able to separate all the HIGH observations from
the 2 other classes. The number of correctly detected HIGH observations would then be
placed in the upper left block in the confusion matrix called ”True HIGH”.
2.6.10
True Negative
A true negative prediction is when the model is able to say that one observation does not
belong to a class. For instance, if the class of interest is HIGH, but the actual class is either
NORMAL or LOW, and the model also was able to predict that it was either NORMAL or
LOW, then it is a true negative observation [27]. Example from the top confusion matrix
in figure 2.15. All the yellow fields are true negative observations where the true class of
the observation is not HIGH and the model did not predict the observation to be HIGH.
2.6.11
False Positive
A false positive observation is an observation that is predicted by the model to belong to
the class of interest but truly the observation is not from the class of interest. That is for
instance if the model predicts an observation to be HIGH, but in fact it is either a NORMAL
or a LOW observation [27]. Example from the top confusion matrix in figure 2.15. The
false positive observations can be seen as the blue fields in the confusion matrix.
2.6.12
False Negative
A false negative observation is an observation that is predicted not to be the class of inter­
est when it actually is in the class of interest [27]. Example from the top confusion matrix
in figure 2.15. The false negative values are the pink fields. If a true HIGH observation is
classified as either a NORMAL or LOW observation, but it actually is a HIGH observation,
it is a false negative observation.

From the confusion matrix many parameters can be calculated, such as precision, sensi­

2.6.13
Precision
Precision is a measure for the quality of the positive predictions, which means out of the
total number of observations predicted to be in a class, how many are correctly classified
[27].
In the WARD hospital setting the precision for the class with:
1. LOW SBP is how many of the predicted LOW alarms that correctly should raise an
alarm.
2. HIGH SBP is how many of the predicted HIGH alarms that correctly should raise an
alarm.
3. NORMAL SBP is how many of the predicted NORMAL observations that correctly
should not raise an alarm.
Precision for HIGH and LOW SBP is important because it tells how many false alarms
that would be given. False alarms are a big problem because it will make the doctor and
nurses waste time on checking patients that are not in a critical condition.
Precision for NORMAL SBP is important because it tells if the model has classified HIGH
or LOW observations to be NORMAL, and thereby miss an alarm that should have been
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
21
==PAGE:30==
raised. If the system does not alert the doctors and nurses when the patients status is
critical then the patients will be in risk of having complications or in worst case dying.
The formula for precision can be seen in formula 2.4.
Precision =
True Positives
True Positives + False Positives
(2.4)
2.6.14
Sensitivity
Sensitivity, also called ”Recall”, tells what proportion of the positive class that was correctly
classified [27]. In the WARD hospital setting the sensitivity for the class with:
• LOW SBP is how many of the LOW SBP alarms that should have been detected
that were actually detected.
• HIGH SBP the sensitivity is how many of the HIGH SBP alarms that should have
been detected that were actually detected.
• NORMAL SBP the sensitivity is how many of the NORMAL SBP values that should
have been detected that were actually detected.
Sensitivity for the LOW and HIGH SBP values is very important because it tells how many
of the patient’s critical SBP values caused an alarm. If the true LOW and HIGH SBP
values do not raise an alarm the doctors and nurses do not know if the patients are in a
dangerous condition. If the patients with abnormal SBP do not get treated in time it can be
life­threatening to the patients. The formula for recall/sensitivity can be seen in 2.5 [27].
Sensitivity =
True Positives
True Positives + False Negative
(2.5)
2.6.15
Specificity
Specificity is the proportion of the true negative class that got correctly classified.
• In the WARD hospital setting the specificity for the LOW SBP values is the proportion
of the true NORMAL and HIGH values that was not classified as LOW.
• For the HIGH SBP values the specificity is the proportion of the true NORMAL and
LOW values that was not classified as HIGH.
• For the NORMAL SBP values the specificity is the proportion of the true LOW and
HIGH values that was not classified as NORMAL [27].
The formula for specificity can be seen in 2.6
Specificity =
True Negative
True Negative + False Positives
(2.6)
2.6.16
Accuracy
Accuracy is calculated as the total number of all correctly classified predictions divided by
the total number of data observations in the data set. It is an overall score for all of the
classes combined. The accuracy can be calculated using equation 2.7 [27].
Accuracy =
True Positives + True Negative
True Positives + True Negative + False Positives + False Negative

2.6.17
F1­score
An F1­score combines precision and sensitivity into on metric, that can be used to com­
pare the performance of 2 classifiers. The F1 score is often used to measure the perfor­
mance of models with unbalanced data sets, for instance if 95% of the data belongs to
one class, and the remaining 5% is distributed between one or more other classes. The
F1 score can be calculated using equation 2.8 [27].
F1 −score = 2 · Precision · Recall
Precision + Recall

2.6.20
Machine learning feature selection
Principal component analysis
Principal component analysis (PCA) is a statistical technique that is used for reducing the
number of features. It is done by projecting the data points into only a few dimensions,
while as much of the variation in the data is still preserved. When the PCA is used, a
percentage is defined, which corresponds to how much of the variation in the data needs
to be preserved. Making a PCA at 100 % will preserve all the principal components, and
by decreasing the retained variance percentage, the number of principal components will
drop. Using PCA in a ML model can reduce the required computational power, reduce
the storage requirements and reduce the risk of overfitting [32].
Analysis of variance
Analysis of Variance (ANOVA) is a statistical method that can be used to select features
by comparing variances across the means within different features. A p­value for each
feature will be calculated, and by defining a certain threshold a group of the most signifi­
cant features can be chosen. Adjusting the threshold will either increase or decrease the
number of features of interest. Excluding the most redundant features can improve the
ML model by making it more simple and faster to train [33] [34].
Maximum Relevance — Minimum Redundancy
Maximum Relevance — Minimum Redundancy (MRMR) is also a feature selection tech­
nique. The selection is based on the correlation between the feature and the response
variable. If for instance 25 features are kept, these are the features with the highest cor­
relation to the response parameter. By reducing the number of irrelevant features, the
ML model can pay more attention to the important features, which both increases the
performance rate and reduces the required computational power and storage [35].
2.7
Regulatories (DS/EN ISO 81060­3:2023)
Before medical devices can be sold to the marked, there are stated requirements in
standards that the device must comply with. The same applies with any kinds of soft­
ware running on a medical device, or software interpreting data within the medical field.
Since this project aims to make a model that should predict SBP and thereby interpret
patient data, the model must comply with certain standards. The standard ”Non­invasive
sphygmomanometers– Part3:Clinical investigation of continuous automated measurement
type (ISO 81060­3:2023)” is a European standard approved by the European committee
for standardization (CEN) [36]. The standard states requirements for continuous BP es­
timation, but only for regression problems. Since this project is a classification problem,
the standard can not be directly used for this project, but it can be used to evaluate former
studies shown in section 3.
2.7.1
Accuracy
The standard does not have any requirements for classification of SBP but it does have
requirements for the accuracy of a regression problem. According to the standard, the
MAE must be less than or equal to 6 mmHg, and the standard diviation (STD) must be
less than or equal to 8 mmHg. These requirements applies for both the SBP and DBP.
2.7.2
Reference method
DS/EN ISO 81060­3:2023 Section 4.3.1 a) states that ”A continuous non­invasive sphyg­
momanometer shall be clinical investigated by using reference invasive BP monitoring
equipment” [36].
2.7.3
Number of subjects
DS/EN ISO 81060­3:2023 Section 4.3.2.2 states that: ”Number of test subjects shall be
greater than or equal to 30 when comparing the performance of the model with measure­
24
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:33==
ments from a reference invasive BP monitoring equipment.”
Gender distribution
DS/EN ISO 81060­3:2023 Section 4.3.2.2 states that:
a) ”At least 30% of the subjects shall be male.
b) At least 30% of the subjects shall be female.”[36]
Age distribution
DS/EN ISO 81060­3:2023 Section 4.3.2.3.2 states the following for age distribution (if the
intended use is for subjects greater than 12 years): ”every subject included in the clinical
investigation shall be greater than 12 years, with the following age distribution:
a) ”40% shall be at least 50 years of age.
b) 25% shall be at least 60 years of age.
c) 10% shall be at least 70 years of age.”[36]
Blood Pressure distribution

3
State of the art within cuff less BP
estimation
This section will dive into previous work within the field of cuff­less BP estimation based
on signals or vital signs measured from the body. For every previous study the purpose,
data, method and results are described.
3.0.1
Yuriy Kurylyak, Francesco Lamonaca, and Domenico Grimaldi, 2013
­ A Neural Network based method for continuous blood pressure
estimation from a PPG signal
Purpose
The purpose of the article ”A Neural Network based method for continuous BP estima­
tion from a PPG signal”[37] is to establish a new continuous, accurate, and noninvasive
method for BP estimation using a feed forward neural network (FNN) with a PPG signal
as input.
Data Description
The data used in the project consists of PPG signals and the corresponding arterial SBP
and DBP measurements from the MIMIC database. Data from 15000 separate heart
beats was used. The data was recorded with a sampling frequency of 125 Hz.
3.0.2
Method
Figure 3.1: Features used to train the FNN
The features used in this model were the duration of systolic and diastolic width of the raw
PPG signal at different levels between baseline and peak, which can be seen in figure 3.1.
26
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:35==
The algorithm used in the project is a Feed­forward Neural Network (FNN) with two hidden
layers, 35 neurons on the first hidden layer and 20 on the second hidden layer.
Figure 3.2: FNN model architecture. x1­xN are the 21 input features from figure 3.1. First
hidden layer has 35 neurons. Second hidden layer has 20 neurons. The model predicts
both SBP and DBP.
Results
The results of the project show that a FFN can be used to accurately estimate SBP and
DBP values from a PPG signal. The model achieved a high accuracy in estimating BP
values, with an MAE of less than 6 mmHg for both SBP and DBP, and therefore the method
complies with the requirements stated in the standard [36].
3.0.3
Kwangsub Song et. al. 2020 ­ Cuffless Deep Learning­Based Blood
Pressure Estimation for Smart Wristwatches
Purpose
The article “Cuffless Deep Learning­Based Blood Pressure Estimation for Smart Wrist­
watches” by Kwangsub Song et. al.[38] uses a deep neural network with ECG and PPG
from smart wristwatches to estimate the BP.
Data Description
Data was collected using a smart wristwatch (InBody smart wristwatch, InBody Corp.,
Seoul, South Korea) and the mercury sphygmomanometer (Desk type 0320, Baumanome­
ter, New York, NY, USA). Data was collected from 110 users.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
27
==PAGE:36==
Method
Figure 3.3: Features
Figure 3.3 show the features that were extracted from the PPG and the ECG.
Figure 3.4: BP estimation using stacked DNN [38]
Figure 3.4 shows how the BP is estimated in the study. Both the PPG and ECG were
collected from the smartwatch. The signals were then preprocessed using a band­pass
Butterworth filter on the PPG and a low­pass Butterworth filter on the ECG signal to re­
move noise. Then features from the ECG and PPG were extracted and feed into a stacked
DNN together with patient body information. Based on this, the BP was estimated.
Results

Purpose
A method for cuffless BP estimation has been examined in the article “Personalized Cuf­

The data used in the study is the same WARD data as described in section 4.1.1.

Based on the HR, RR, SpO2, and PR the BP was estimated using a random forest algo­

Figure 3.5: BP estimation using random forest

and the ECG device. Then the first three hours of the data from each patient is used
for personalized calibration using the mean, STD, and range of each parameter. The
features mean, STD, and range are calculated from PPG and ECG data. All features,
both calibration and normal features from the first day the patient was monitored, is then
used to train a random forest model that predicts the BP value. The model has been
validated on the data from second day from each patient.

3.0.6
Shrimanti Ghosh et al, 2016 ­ Continuous blood pressure prediction
from pulse transit time using ECG and PPG signals
Purpose
The Article ”Continuous blood pressure prediction from pulse transit time using ECG and
PPG signals” [40] examines pulse transit time (PTT) as a non­invasive method for contin­
uous BP estimation. BP was estimated using PTT where the patient was in five different
positions (recumbent, seated, standing, walking, cycling) according to a preset protocol.
Data Description
The data used in the study came from fourteen healthy subjects (8 females and 6 males).
The data was acquired using the BioRadio (Great Lakes Neurotechnologies, Valley View,
OH) device. From the device, three channels of ECG and one channel of PPG were
captured and wireless streamed using built­in software BioCapture. BP was measured
using the oscillometric device (WatchBP Office, Micro Life, Widnau, Switzerland) as the
reference standard.
3.0.7
Method
Figure 3.6: PTT [41]
The PTT was calculated using the time delay between the ECG and the PPG. The PTT
is defined as the time between the R­peak of the ECG and the systolic peak from the
PPG measured within the same cardiac cycle. This is shown in figure 3.6. The PTT is a
measure of the time it takes the pulse pressure waveform to propagate through a length of
the arterial tree. The PTT was computed using the windowed correlation between ECG
and PPG signals. Continuous BP was estimated using the following linear regression
model:
BP =
a
PTT + b
(3.1)
30
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:39==
Where a and b are subject­dependent parameters that are calculated using a least square
algorithm during calibration.
Figure 3.7: PTT BP estimation process flow
Figure 3.7 shows how the BP was estimated using the PTT. First BP was measured using
a BP cuff device for calibration. Then the ECG and PPG were measured. The PTT value
was calculated and a calibration is done based on the BP and PTT value to determine the
subject­dependent parameters a and b. Then the BP was estimated using only the PTT.
Results

Seated
Standing
Walking

dard deviation)
According to DS/EN ISO 81060–3:2023 both in the seated and standing position the PTT
SBP measurement was within the acceptable accuracy of 6 mmHg ± a STD of 8 mmHg
[36].
3.0.8
Monika Simjanoska et. Al. 2018 ­ Noninvasive blood pressure
estimation from ECG using machine learning techniques
Purpose
In the article ”Noninvasive blood pressure estimation from ECG using machine learning
techniques”, the purpose was to estimate the SBP and DBP using machine learning inter­
preting and ECG signal. The ECG signal was filtered and segmented, followed by feature
extraction. The features were used as input for a ML method and a regression module.
The model was then evaluated on test data. [30]
Data Description
In this study, the data consisted of ECG segments and the corresponding BP value. In
total 51 patients of different ages participated in the experiment. In total, 3129 30­seconds
ECG segments were collected. The data was divided into a training set and a test set to
evaluate the performance of the ML model.
Method
The ML method used in this project is a combination of a stacking­based classification
and a regression model. The stacking­based classification is a technique where several
ML algorithms are trained and gathered in a meta­model, which is an algorithm that learns
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
31
==PAGE:40==
from other learning algorithms. A sketch of a stacking­based classification architecture
can be seen in figure 3.8 [42]. The ML models used in the stacking model is a KNN, J48,
Naive Bayes, SVM, Random Forest, Bagging, and Boosting.
Figure 3.8: Block diagram of a possible stacking based model architecture [42]
Ultimately, a regression was applied, which was used to determine absolute values for
the SBP and DBP.
Results
The results showed that the model achieved a MAE for SBP at 8.64 mmHg ± 10.74
mmHg, and a RMSE for SBP at 10.97 mmHg. For DBP the model achieved a MAE at 18.2
mmHg ± 8.45 mmHg and a RMSE at 19.34 mmHg. To increase the model performance a
calibration was added, which made the model specific to the individual patient. However,
the performance of the model was out of the acceptance range but can be a base for
further investigation within this field.
3.0.9
Thomas, Simi Susan et. al, 2016 ­ BioWatch: A Noninvasive
Wrist­Based Blood Pressure Monitor That Incorporates Training
Techniques for Posture and Subject Variability
Purpose
In the article “BioWatch: A Noninvasive Wrist­Based Blood Pressure Monitor That Incor­
porates Training Techniques for Posture and Subject Variability” by Thomas, Simi Susan
et. al.[43] a solution to measure BP using PTT calculated using PPG and ECG has been
examined.
Data Description
The data were obtained from 11 healthy subjects using a device made in the lab, the Em­
bedded Signal Processing Lab (ESP), in partnership with Texas Instruments (TI) called
BioWatch for the PTT measurements. As a reference device, the arterial BP Colin Con­
tinuous BP Monitor, CBM­7000, was used.
Method
The method is the same as described in section 3.0.6 except the ECG and PPG values
comes from a watch and the reference device is a arterial BP device instead of a cuff­
device.
Results
The average RMSE between the measured true systolic BP and the calculated systolic
BP was between 7.83 mmHg and 9.37 mmHg on the 11 subjects. For the diastolic BP
the RMSE was between 5.77 mmHg to 6.90 mmHg. With these results the study did not
comply with the standard [36].

Mohamad Kachuee et. al, 2015 ­ Cuff­less high­accuracy
calibration­free blood pressure estimation using pulse transit time
Purpose
The article “Cuff­less high­accuracy calibration­free blood pressure estimation using pulse
transit time” by Mohamad Kachuee et. al.[44] focuses on making a ML algorithm that can
predict the BP. The input to the model is physiological parameters from the PPG signal.
Data Description
The data used in the project was from the MIMIC II database were thousands of signals
are recorded from patients at different hospitals in the time period from 2001 and 2008.
The data contains PPG, ECG and BP segments sampled at 125 HZ.
Method
Different algorithms were tested, such as a regularized linear regression, an artificial neu­
ral network, and a support vector machine (SVM), to see which one would perform best.
The method follows the structure as seen in figure 3.9
Figure 3.9: Block diagram of BP estimating ML algorithm [44]

for the DBP, and 12.38 ±16.17 for the SBP. However, the model did not perform good
enough to comply with the DS/EN ISO 81060–3:2023 [36].

==PAGE:42==
Learning from state­of­the­art analysis
To sum up, the examined articles provide deep insights into the field of cuff­less BP esti­
mation using ECG and PPG signals or vital signs. Each study explores different methods,
approaches or features for continuous cuff­less BP estimation, utilizing various devices
and algorithms.
Overall, these studies contribute to the advancement of continuous BP estimation tech­
niques. While some approaches show promising results, further research and improve­
ments are needed to achieve precise and reliable cuff­less BP estimation in clinical sce­
narios. The studies can therefore be used for inspiration for other projects in the field of
BP estimation.

==PAGE:43==
4
Methodology
Figure 4.1: Process flow for the data
The data used for the model developed in this project are the four WARD data sets: WARD
Observational Surgery, WARD Observational COPD, WARD RCT medical and WARD
RCT Surgery. Combined these four data sets include 875 patients from where a balanced
training set of 540 (180 LOW, 180 NORMAL, and 180 HIGH) HR, RR, SpO2, PR, and
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
35
==PAGE:44==
SBP values. These vital signs are recorded once every minute. Besides the vital signs,
10 seconds of continuous data from the ECG and PPG device are also included. These
segments are sampled at 75 Hz.
Figure 4.1 shows how the data have been processed to make the model. First data have
been loaded and combined in Matlab. Then outliers described in table 4.2 have been
removed from the data. Then data have been split in a training, validation and test set
with 80 % of the data in the training set, 10% of the data in the validation set, and 10
% of the data in the test set. The true class for each observation called ”BP state” was
calculated using the criteria from table 1.1. Then 150 different features are calculated.
These 150 features are shown in appendix A.0.2. The training set is balanced to have an
equal amount of LOW, NORMAL and HIGH SBP observations. The 49 vital sign features
are used to train a random forest model with 100 trees, a maximum number of splits of
2000. This model was used to predict the LOW observations. The 101 segment features
are used to train a random forest model with 100 trees, a maximum number of splits of
2000. The models are tested and optimized based on the validation set. This model was
used to predict the HIGH observations. The two models are combined into one model
where the HIGH­model is predicting if a SBP is HIGH and the LOW­model is predicting
if the SBP is LOW. Post processing is applied where the observation before and after
the observation of interest are analysed to adjust the prediction of the model. The post­
processing adjustments can be seen in table 4.4. Finally the model is tested using the
test set with an accuracy of 74.5%.
4.1
Data
In this project five different data sets have been used. Four different data sets from the
WARD project and one data set from the MIMIC III database. The four WARD data sets
will be considered as 1 data set in table 4.1.
Data set

Table 4.1: WARD and MIMIC data. The table is to give an overview of the data sets
and how the data is distributed within the data sets. The definition for a HIGH, LOW and
NORMAL SBP is defined in table 1.1.
4.1.1
WARD Observational Surgery
The data comes from an observational study including 500 postoperative cancer patients,
who all have been through a major abdominal surgery. The patients were monitored up
to four days after the surgery. All the data were obtained at Rigshospitalet and Bispebjerg
Hospital in Copenhagen, Denmark, as a part of the WARD project.
During the data collection, the patients were monitored with a single lead ECG patch
(Lifetouch Blue), which read the HR and the RR, both with one measurement per minute
36
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:45==
and a 10 second raw ECG signal sampled at 75 Hz collected for every minute. The SpO2
and pulse Rate were measured once every minute and a 10 second raw PPG signal
sampled at 75 Hz was collected every minute with a pulse oximeter (Nonin WristOx2).
The SBP was measured every 30 minutes during day time, and once every hour during
the night time. The SBP measurements were obtained using a cuff­based blood pressure
monitor, TM­2441, around the upper arm. From the devices, the data were transmitted to
a server (Isansys Patient Status Engine), from where it is accessible [45].
4.1.2
WARD Observational COPD
The WARD observational COPD trial is a study with 200 participants conducted at Bis­
pebjerg Hospital in Copenhagen, Denmark. The patients were monitored with the WARD
setup from admission and up to four days. All patients included were at the age of 18 or
older, and all were suffering from COPD [46]. The data was collected in the same way as
described in section 4.1.1.
4.1.3
WARD RCT Medical
The WARD Randomized Clinical Trial (RCT) is a study with 300 participants conducted at
Rigshospitalet, Bispebjerg Hospital, Odense University Hospital, and Aalborg University
Hospital in Denmark. The patients were monitored with the WARD setup described in
section 4.1.1. All patients included were at the age of 18 or older, and all were defined as
high­risk patients [47]. The data was collected in the same way as described in section
4.1.1.
4.1.4
WARD RCT Surgery
The WARD RCT Surgery data is from a study with 400 participants conducted at Rigshos­
pitalet, Bispebjerg Hospital, Odense University Hospital, Aalborg University Hospital in
Denmark. The patients were monitored with the WARD setup described in section 4.1.1.
All patients included were at the age of 18 or older, and all were undergoing a major
vascular­, urological­, orthopedic­ or abdominal surgery [48]. The data was collected in
the same way as described in section 4.1.1.
4.1.5
All WARD data combined
Figure 4.2 shows boxplots of the distribution of vital signs from the WARD data.
For the HR it can be seen that most of the data are in the normal range from 60 BPM to
100 BPM [6]. The lowest observed HR is 0 BPM and the highest is 283 BPM.
The RR boxplot shows that around 50 % of the data are within the normal range of 12
BrPM to 20 BrPM [6]. The lowest observed value is 0 and the highest observed value is
78 BrPM.
The SpO2 boxplot shows that around 25 % of the data are within the normal range of 96
% to 100 % [6]. The lowest observed value is 2 % and the highest observed value is 100
%.
The PR boxplot shows that around 60 % of the data are within the normal range of 60
BPM to 100 BPM [6]. The lowest observed value is 0 BPM and the highest observed
value is 343 BPM.
The SBP boxplot shows that around 50 % of the data are within the normal range of 90
mmHg to 140 mmHg [6]. The lowest observed value is 0 mmHg and the highest observed
value is 343 mmHg.

4.1.6
MIMIC III
MIMIC III (Medical Information Mart for Intensive Care III) is a freely available database
that contains health­related data of patients who were admitted to the intensive care units
(ICUs) at the Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center (BIDMC) in Boston, Massachusetts,
USA, between 2001 and 2012. The database contains large amounts clinical data of more
than 58,000 patients, including vital signs, laboratory test results, medication orders, pro­
cedures, diagnosis codes, and survival outcomes. The data is organized in tables, and
each patient is assigned a unique identifier that allows the data to be linked across different
tables. The MIMIC III database has been widely used in research studies related to crit­
ical care medicine, such as predicting patient outcomes, evaluating treatment protocols,
and developing machine learning algorithms for clinical decision­making. Of the around
58.000 patients in the database, nearly 18.000 of them had their vital signs recorded, and
therefore it was decided to test the MIMIC III database to train a model for SBP estimation.
4.2
Data Processing
4.2.1
Process Flow
Figure 4.1 shows how the data is processed in Matlab. At first each step in the process
was optimized based on making one model predicting three classes. But after optimizing
the model it was found that the vital sign features were very good at predicting LOW SBP
and the segment features was very good at predicting HIGH SBP why a combination
model was made.
40
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:49==
WARD Process
Figure 4.5: Flow of how all Matlab scripts used to process WARD data are connected.
Input and output from each function are shown on the arrows.
The data was loaded into Matlab where it was rearranged followed by removal of outliers.
After outliers were removed, the data was split into test, training, and validation set. In­
terpolation of the vital signs was tested but not applied in the final model. Then the SBP
state was found, which is the parameter the model has to predict. After the SBP state
was found, the features were calculated and extracted from the vital signs and segments.
Before training the model, the training set was balanced to have an equal distribution of
HIGH, LOW and NORMAL SBP states. Then the HIGH­model was trained on the segment
features from the training set and the LOW­model was trained on the vital sign features
from training set. Both models were tested on the validation set. The hyper parameters
were optimized to increase the performance of the model. When the model was trained
and optimized, post processing was applied to increase specificity of the model. Finally
the model was tested on the test set to make sure that the model was not over­fitted to
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
41
==PAGE:50==
the validation set. The main script used to process the WARD data in Matlab can be seen
in appendix A.0.18. Figure 4.5 show how the Matlab scripts used to process WARD data
are linked.
4.2.2
MIMIC Vital Signs Process
The MIMIC Vital Signs data have been processed in the same way as the WARD LOW­
model. The only difference was that the data was not split in test, training and validation
because the WARD test and validation set were used to evaluate how the model performs
on WARD data. The Matlab script ”MIMIC_MAIN.m” that can be seen in appendix A.0.30
was used to process the MIMIC data.
4.2.3
Rearrange data
Figure 4.6: Data rearranged in Matlab
All data are stored in CSV files with data from one patient in each CSV file. The CSV files
are loaded into Matlab and put into a cell array where data from each patient is put in its
cell. By doing this it is more convenient to process each patient individually. The process
can be seen in figure 4.6. The WARD Segment data are stored in PKL files why they
were first processed in Python where each file was converted to a CSV file, this is done
in the Python scrip in Appendix A.0.31. The MIMIC data was in one CSV file but was still
processed in the same way where each patient where put in one cell. The WARD vital
sign data was loaded and rearrange using the Matlab code seen in appendix A.0.19. The
WARD segment data was loaded and rearrange using the Matlab code seen in appendix
A.0.20.
4.2.4
Combine data
The WARD Vital Sign data and the WARD Segments data were not located in the same
files why they had to be combined. The data was combined using Matlabs join function
and the data was combined if the rows had the same patient ID and timestamp. Some of
the patients only had vital sign data or segment data recorded, why the data set became
smaller after the combination of the data. The Matlab code used to combine the segment
and vital sign data can be seen in appendix A.0.24.
42
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs

4.2.5

Before the data could be used, outliers had to be removed. Removing the outliers was
done by using the Matlab function ”removeOutliers2”, which can be found in appendix
A.0.21. The function finds the extreme values and replaces them with a ”NaN”. Extreme
values are defined as values outside the range specified in table 4.2. Values outside this
range are coursed by artefacts and are unrealistic to detect from patients. The boundaries
have been empirically defined by doctors from the WARD project. See Appendix A.0.3
for the mail correspondence where these values are defined.

4.2.6
Split in test, training and validation
After the outliers were removed, the data set was split into a training set, a validation set
and a test set. The training set contains the data that the ML model is trained on, while
the validation set is used to test the model performance for hyper parameter optimization.
Every time a parameter or feature has been optimized, the validation set is used to see if
the performance has increased or decreased. When all the parameters and features were
optimized on the validation set, the test set is applied. The model has at this point never
seen the data from the test set, and therefore this test can be considered representative
of data directly from a patient. If the model was both validated and tested on the validation
set, the model could struggle with over­fitting, because the parameters and features likely
could have been tuned to this specific data. Figure 4.8 shows how the data is split into
different data sets. 80% of the patients goes into the WARD training set, while 10 % goes
into the validation set and last 10 % goes to the test set. Both the validation set and the
test set were not balanced to make the data look as real as possible.
Figure 4.8: Showing how the data is distributed to the training set, the validation set and
the test set
44
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:53==
4.2.7
Linear Interpolation
During daytime there was 30 minutes between every SBP measurement, and in the night
time the gap was 60 minutes. If the devices are correctly placed on the patients, there will
be one observation recorded every minute, but artefacts or missing values can occur, and
thereby gabs for a longer time duration in these records can occur as well. Interpolation is
a way to generate more data between the original data. The interpolation method tested
in this project was a linear interpolation. To make the linear interpolation on the data,
a Matlab function was made. The function is called ”Interpolation4” and can be found in
appendix A.0.23. The function finds the number of empty cells between two observations.
If there is less than one hour between the two observations, the function makes a linear
line between the two observations, and makes a new data point on the line where there
are empty timestamps. An example on that can be seen in figure 4.9. The upper scatter
plot shows the SBP measurement for the first three hours of monitoring one of the WARD
patients. The lower scatter plot shows how linear interpolation was applied between the
SBP measurements. The original points are the red circles, while all the blue circles are
synthetic points. From these plots it is clear that the interpolation leads to a larger training
set.
Figure 4.9: Non interpolated and interpolated SBP. The upper graph shows the SBP in
mmHg on the Y­axis and the time on the x­axis. The non­interpolated SBP values are
plotted with red circles. The bottom plot shows the same SBP values as on the upper
graph together with linear interpolated data points. One new interpolated sample has
been made for every minute without a SBP sample.
If there is more then one hour between two data points the linear interpolation is not made.
This is because the SBP can change a lot within a few hours, why it would be too imprecise
if two points several hours apart are connected with a straight line.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
45
==PAGE:54==
4.2.8
Find SBP state
Figure 4.10: SBP State according to table 1.1
The SBP state was calculated using different ranges. The SBP states used in the final
model is LOW, NORMAL, or HIGH according to the ranges defined in table 1.1. This is
calculated using the function ”BPState 4” function seen in Appendix A.0.22. The func­
tion adds an extra column to the patient data table called ”BPS_State1” with the values
”LOW”, ”NORMAL” or ”HIGH”. The output can be seen in figure 4.10. Tabel 1.1 indi­
cates that there needs at least two continuous observations in the light red area (between
180 and 220 mmHg) to make the observation HIGH, and in the same way there needs
at least two continuous observations in the light red area (between 91 and 70 mmHg)
to make the observation LOW. Looking in the light red area in figure 4.10 both blue and
red observations occur. The blue dot observations corresponds to the observations that
are defined as NORMAL, while the red triangles corresponds to observations classified
as either HIGH or LOW. NORMAL observation occurs in the light red area because the
previous observation either is normal, or if the time span to the previous observation is
46
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:55==
too long. In the daytime there must be less than 31 minutes between the observations,
and in night time there must be less than 61 minutes.
Another range that was tested was: LOW SBP < 117 mmHg NORMAL SBP < 136 mmHg
HIGH SBP. This range was tested to try to make the classes with HIGH and LOW obser­
vations larger. With these boundaries approximately 1/3 of the data would be in the LOW
class, 1/3 in the NORMAL class, and 1/3 of the data in the HIGH class. This SBP state
was calculated using the function ”BPStatesimple” seen in appendix A.0.22.
The SBP state was also tested as a two class problem where all LOW and HIGH values
were combined in one class called ABNORMAL, where the LOW, NORMAL and HIGH
values were found based on the criteria from table 1.1. This SBP state was calculated
using the function ”table_binary_class2” seen in appendix A.0.22.
4.3
Feature selection
Training a ML algorithm requires features in order to make a classification. The fea­
tures are calculated from the training set, validation set, and test set, and are used to
extract information from the raw data that the model can interpret to make a prediction.
The features are calculated using the three Matlab functions ”Segments_Features.m”,
”Find_features_Personal_segment_plus_vital2.m” and ”pan_tompkin.m”.
The function
”pan_tompkin.m” comes directly from the project described in [49] made by Hooman
Sedghamiz. The Matlab code for the three functions can be seen in appendix A.0.2.
