Figure 4.11 shows how the vital signs features are only calculated when there are 29
minutes of continues data from all four modalities (HR, RR, SpO2 and PR). The figure
shows how the features are calculated based on all the data points 29 min before. It can
be seen that data from HR and RR is available more than two hours before the features
are calculated. This is because the PPG device has not been applied to the patient in this
period. Only when at least nine data points from all four modalities are available within
the last 29 minutes the features are calculated. When adding the segment features, at
the timestamp where a SBP measurement is present, the PPG and ECG segment must
contain at least 18 samples. If there is less than 18 samples in the segment, the features
will not be calculated. Figure 4.13 indicates that there needs at least 9 minutes of data
out of 29 in the time span before a SBP for the features to be calculated.
Figure 4.13: data required to calculate features. In the 29 minutes before a SBP measure­
ment there must be at least nine SpO2, PR, RR, and HR values recorded simultaneously.
From the same minute as the SBP there must be one ECG and one PPG segment with at
least 18 samples. In the figure it is marked as the first 9 minutes that contains data. It is
not required that it is the first nine minutes that contains data, nor it is required that the nine
minutes are continues sampled. The nine minutes could as well have been distributed all
over the 29 minutes.
Features segment data
The following features have been extracted from the ECG segment data:
• R­peak amplitude: mean, STD, min, max
• RR­Interval: mean, STD
• RMSSD
• SDNN
• QRS Area: mean, STD, min, max
• QR­slope: mean, STD, min, max
• RS­slope: mean, STD, min, max
• QRS Duration: mean, STD, min, max
50
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:59==
R­peak amplitude refers to the amplitude of the R­peak, and RR­interval refers to the time
between two R­peaks.
RMSSD stands for ’Root Mean Square of Successive Differences’ and quantifies the
amount of variability in the beat­to­beat intervals when analyzing the HR variability (HRV).
The equation for RMSSD can be seen in equation 4.5 [54].
RMSSD =
"#N−1
i=1 (RRi −RRi+1)2
N −1
(4.5)
Where N is number of samples. RRi is the individual RR interval.
SDNN refers to the STD of the Normal to Normal interbeat interval. The equation for
SDNN can be seen in equation 4.6 [55].
SDNN =
"#N
i=1(RRi −RR)2
N −1
(4.6)
Where N is number of samples. RRi is the individual RR interval. RR is the sample mean
of the RR intervals.
The QRS area corresponds to the area delimited by the Q, R, and S peaks. It can be
seen as the light red area on figure 4.15.
The QR­slope and are the average slopes between the Q­ and R­peak. It can be seen as
the turquoise dashed line in figure 4.15.
The RS­slope is the average slopes between the R­ and S peaks. It can be seen as the
pink dashed line in figure 4.15.
QRS duration is the time between the Q­peak and S­peak. It can be seen as the black
dashed line in figure 4.15.
The following features have been extracted from the PPG segment data:
• Systolic­peak amplitude: mean, STD, min, max
• IBI: mean, STD
• RMSSD
• SDNN
• Systolic­peak Area: mean, STD, min, max
• Onset/systolic­slope: mean, STD, min, max
• Systolic/notch­slope: mean, STD, min, max
• Cycle Duration: mean, STD, min, max
Systolic­peak amplitude refers to the amplitude of the systolic peak.
IBI stands for Inter Beat Interval and refers to the time between two systolic peaks.
RMSSD quantifies the amount of variability in the beat­to­beat intervals. The RMSSD for
the PPG is calculated using equation 4.5, which is the same equation as used for the ECG
but instead of the RR interval the IBI is used.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
51
==PAGE:60==
SDNN refers to the STD of the IBIs and is calculated using the same equation as for the
ECG 4.6 but instead of the RR interval the IBI is used.
The systolic peak area corresponds to the area below the systolic peak. It can be seen
as the light red area on figure 4.17.
The onset/systolic slope is the average slope between the onset­ and systolic peak. It
can be seen as the turquoise dashed line in figure 4.17.
The systolic/notch slope is the average slope between the systolic­ and dicrotic notch
peak. It can be seen as the pink dashed line in figure 4.17.
Cycle duration is the time between the onset­peak and dicrotic notch­peak. It can be seen
as the black dashed line in figure 4.17.
Peak detection
The Pan­Tompkins algorithm has been used to detect the R­peak on the ECG signal and
the systolic top peak of the PPG signal. To find the Q peak and S peak the derivative of
the signal is analysed to see when the sign of the derivative signal goes from negative to
positive for the Q peak, and from negative to positive from the S peak. The same applies
for the PPG signal when detecting the onset peak and the dicrotic notch.
Figure 4.14: ECG features from one patient in the WARD segment data.
Figure 4.14 shows how one ECG segment looks when it has been band pass filtered
together with the features that are used in the ML model. In the 9.5 seconds that the
segment lasted, 13 QRS­complexes were found.
52
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:61==
Figure 4.15: Features from ECG segments
Figure 4.15 shows a close up QRS­complex where the calculated features are marked.
The Q­, R­ and S­peak are found, and the average slope between the Q­ and R­peak and
the average slope between the R­ and S­peak are calculated. Both slopes are marked with
dotted lines. The area under the QRS­complex and the QRS time duration are calculated.
Figure 4.16: Features from 10 seconds PPG segment
Figure 4.16 shows one filtered PPG segment together with the calculated features.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
53
==PAGE:62==
Figure 4.17: Features from one PPG segment peak
Figure 4.16 shows a close up of a band pass filtered PPG­wave where the features that
have been calculated are marked. The onset peak, the systolic peak and the dicrotic
notch are found, and the average slope between the onset peak and systolic peak and the
average slope between the systolic peak and dicrotic notch are calculated. Both slopes
are marked with dotted lines. The time duration of a full PPG­wave (from onset peak to
dicrotic notch) are calculated and marked with a black dotted line.
Time before SBP
The features of the final model are calculated using the previous 29 minutes data obser­
vations before the SBP measurement. The vital sign values closest to the SBP value
must be considered as the values with the highest correlation to the SBP value. How­
ever, having too few values to calculate the features can be imprecise if just one of the
values is missing. Therefore, the period that the features are based and calculated on is a
parameter that have been optimized. Hereinafter, this period will be referred to as ”Time
before SBP”.
Accepted number of NaN
It occurs that some of the data from the devices are missing, either due to the devices not
being connected to the patient or errors in the readings. In the perfect data set data from
all the modalities would be present at the same time, which is not the case with the WARD
or the MIMIC data used in this project. Therefore, an accepted amount of missing values
(called ”accepted number of NAN”) in the time before SBP is defined. For instance, if the
time before SBP is defined as 29 minutes, and the accepted number of NAN is defined
as 20 values, it means that at least nine of the rows/minutes must include data from all
modalities.
Number of calibration values
In an attempt to make the ML model more personal, calibration values were added. The
calibration values were all the features explained above combined with the mean, STD,
minimum, maximum, range, and skewness for the SBP, extracted in the time span from the
54
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:63==
first monitored measurement to the time when the patient have the sixth SBP measured.
These calibration features are for the model to learn how the features for the individual
patient look in the beginning of the monitoring period. Having many calibration values
means a longer delay before the model can start predicting SBP, but the model learns
more about the specific patient.
4.3.3
Balance data
After the features have been extracted and calculated, the data is ready to be used for
training. However, the data set is highly unbalanced with the major part of the observations
in the NORMAL state. Figure 4.18 shows how the data is distributed within the three
classes. The unbalanced data makes the model very good at predicting patients in the
NORMAL state, but almost impossible for the model to predict patients in the HIGH or
LOW state. This is because the model only has a small opportunity to learn how the
features are when the SBP state is either HIGH or LOW. The Matlab function used to
balance the data can be seen in A.0.27.
Figure 4.18: Unbalanced data set
Balanced in 2 classes
In this training set all HIGH and LOW SBP state observations are combined in one class
called ”ABNORMAL”. This is done to make the prediction more simple. This balance of
data with a 2­class problem will result in a training set size of 1094 observations where
547 of them are ABNORMAL, and the remaining 547 are NORMAL. From figure 4.19 it
can be seen how the distribution of HIGH, LOW and NORMAL data will be with the data
balanced this way.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
55
==PAGE:64==
Figure 4.19: Pie chart for the distribution of the WARD data set balanced in two equal
sized classes, ABNORMAL (HIGH and LOW) and NORMAL
Balanced in 3 classes
In this data set the data is divided in HIGH, NORMAL and LOW SBP states. All three
classes have equal amount of data. Since the HIGH class is the smallest, this is the one
that determines the class size to be 180 observations per class. The total amount of data
in this data set is 540. All the HIGH observation from the training set is used and then
only 180 observations randomly selected from the LOW and NORMAL observations are
used. The distribution of data can be seen in figure 4.20.
Figure 4.20: Pie chart for the distribution of the WARD data set balanced in three equal
sized classes, HIGH, NORMAL and LOW
Balanced in skew class
In order to keep as much data as possible, this skew distribution is introduced. Here all
the HIGH and LOW observations are included since these states are important to detect.
The number of NORMAL SBP state observations included here corresponds to the largest
of the HIGH/LOW SBP state classes, which here is the LOW SBP state. To sum up, this
training set consists of 180 HIGH SBP state observations and 367 observations of both
LOW and NORMAL SBP states. The 512 NORMAL observations are randomly selected
out of all the NORMAL observations in the training set. The distribution can be seen in
figure 4.21.
56
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:65==
Figure 4.21: Pie chart for the distribution of the WARD data set in skew class
4.4
Training, testing and validating
4.4.1
Train model
To train the random forest classifier the classification learner app in Matlab was used. In
this app a training set can be selected from the workspace. Then a response variable
can be selected together with the predictors. After selecting the desired classification
algorithm, in this case the random forest, the training can be started. The two Matlab
functions ”trainClassifier_vital.m” and ”trainClassifier_segments” seen in appendix A.0.28
are used to train the two models used in the combined model. The functions are made
by exporting the functions made in the Matlab classification learner app.
4.4.2
Test model
After training the random forest, the classification learner app makes a 5­fold cross­
validation on the training set. To evaluate the model on data that the model has not been
trained on, the validation set and test set is loaded into the classification app. Then the
model predicts the SBP class and compares it with the true class and from this a confusion
matrix with the result is made.
4.4.3
Optimize model hyper parameters
The number of trees, maximum number of splits, and misclassification cost described in
section 2.6.3 are optimized using the Matlab Classification Learner application. The hyper
parameters are applied before training the model. Hyper parameters are optimized based
on the model performance on the training set and the validation set.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
57
==PAGE:66==
4.4.4
Combined model
Figure 4.22: Combination model. The flow to the left shows how the combination model
makes its classification if the HIGH­model predicts HIGH and the LOW­model predicts
NORMAL. The Flow in the middle shows how the combination model makes its classifi­
cation if the HIGH­model predicts NORMAL and the LOW­model predicts LOW. The flow
to the right shows how the combination model makes its classification if the HIGH­model
predicts HIGH and the LOW­model predicts LOW.
Making one model that is good at predicting both LOW, NORMAL and HIGH SBP was not
possible. However, it was found that a combination of two models was better than one
model trying to predict all three states. Initially a model (LOW­model) that was very good
at predicting LOW SBP was trained. Then a model (HIGH­model) that was very good at
predicting HIGH SBP was trained. Then these two models were combined to one, so all
the SBP classified as LOW by the LOW­model was defined as LOW. Then all the SBP
classified as HIGH by the HIGH­model was defined as HIGH. Finally, all the remaining
SBP were defined as NORMAL. This way each model (HIGH­model and LOW­model)
only predicts the class that the model is good at predicting. If the LOW­model predicts
one sample to be LOW and the HIGH­model predicts the same observation to be HIGH
then the observation will be classified as HIGH by the combined model. This can be seen
in figure 5.7 and was chosen because it gave the best performance of the combined model
on the validation set. The combination model is made using the Matlab code in the main
script ”WARD_combined_model_MAIN.m” that can be seen in appendix A.0.18. The two
Matlab functions ”trainClassifier_vital.m” and ”trainClassifier_segments” seen in appendix
A.0.28 are used to train the HIGH­model and the LOW­model.
4.4.5
Post processing
In order to increase the performance of the combined model, post­processing has been
applied. The Matlab function ”combi_and_post.m” seen in appendix A.0.29 has been
used to make the post processing.
In this project 6 different post processing adjustments have been applied to the combined
model. Table 4.4 shows the 6 different adjustments
58
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:67==

Table 4.4: Post processing adjustments. Each line shows one adjustment for a specific
combination of predictions that are applied in the post processing. The adjustment are
only applied if there are less than 300 minutes between the observation before and the
observation after the observation of interest.
Adjustment number:
1. If the model predicts an observation to be LOW, and the observation before and
after are NORMAL then observation should be NORMAL
2. If the model predicts an observation to be HIGH, and the observation before and
after are NORMAL then the observation should be NORMAL
3. If the model predicts an observation to be LOW, and the observation after are HIGH
then the observation should be NORMAL
4. If the model predicts an observation to be HIGH, and the observation after are low
then the observation should be NORMAL
5. If the model predicts an observation to be LOW, and the observation before is HIGH
then the observation should be NORMAL
6. If the model predicts an observation to be HIGH, and the observation before is LOW
then the observation should be NORMAL
These rules are made to increase the precision by decreasing the number of false pos­
itive NORMAL values. Besides increasing the precision it is also considered unrealistic
that the SBP goes from HIGH to LOW or from LOW to HIGH within less than one hour.
Having a single HIGH or a single LOW observation surrounded by NORMAL observations
is considered negligible and that will therefore be made to a NORMAL observation.
4.4.6
Test final model
When the model is optimized and the post processing has been applied, the model is
ready to be tested on the test set to test if the model has been over fitted to the training
and validation set.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
59
==PAGE:68==
5
Results
In this section the results from tests of features and hyper parameters described in section
4 are shown. The features are optimized to one model that predicts all three classes, since
it was late in the process that the idea to a combined model was discovered.
The random forest classifier was the best ML model. The best model was made with no
interpolation of the data, and with the SBP state defined as in table 1.1. For the features
the model performed best with 20 accepted NaN values, 29 minutes of data before a SBP
measurement and 6 calibration values. Both the vital signs and segments with and without
calibration values were tested separately, where it turned out that using both the vital signs
and the segments, both with calibration values, gave the best results. The different data
distributions were tested, and it showed that the 3­class distribution was best. Both the
PCA, the ANOVA, and the MRMR analysis showed that the best results were achieved
when all the features were included in the model. The results during hyperparameter
tuning showed that the best results were achieved with 100 trees, a maximum of 2000
splits, and a misclassification cost applied as shown in figure 5.4.
5.1
Select ML Model
Before training, a classification algorithm had to be selected. To make the selection, all
models from the Matlab ”Classification Learner application” were tested. This includes
nearest neighbor classifiers, ensemble classifiers, decision tree classifiers, artificial neu­
ral networks, and support vector machines. From the test of all these classifiers, it was
concluded that it was a random forest classifier that gave the best results and is the clas­
sification method used in the final model. The data set used for this test was the WARD
vital signs plus segments data. With 5 accepted NaN values for the previous 29 minutes
of data before an SBP measurement and six SBP values used for calibration. All 150
features were used.
Figure 5.1 shows the results from the test of the random forest, figure 5.2 shows the
results from the test with a fine gaussian SVM classifier, and figure 5.3 shows the results
from the test with a coarse tree classifier. These three tests were ones with best results.
All results from these tests can be found in appendix A.0.17.
The results table as the one showed in figure 5.1 is to provide information about the test
and show the most common metrics within ML evaluation. The first two rows explain
which settings the test are based on. The third row is a row of headers, where each
header corresponds to the below standing column. After the headers, the 5­fold cross
validation is presented. From the left is the confusion matrix with the true class on the y­
axis and the predicted class on the x­axis, and then each of the columns shows the metrics
mentioned in the header row. In some of the columns there are three rows, corresponding
to the classes (HIGH, LOW, NORMAL), which can be seen on the left side of the confusion
matrix. If only one cell is shown, it is because the metric is an overall metric for all three
classes. In some cases one or more of the values are substituted with #####. This is if
one of the values in the confusion matrix is zero, which then results in a division with zero,
and therefore no value can be calculated.
60

Figure 5.3: Results from test of Coarse Tree classifier
5.1.1
Evaluation parameters of interest
There are several ways to evaluate a confusion matrix. Since this project is within health
care, one alarm too much is better then a missed alarm. Therefore, initially a high Recal­
l/sensitivity is of interest for all prediction classes, also at the cost that many false positive
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs

predictions will occur. In the following section a result table as the one used in table 5.1
will be used to evaluate the tests. The first column in the table is used to describe which
test is being tested. The second, third and fourth column corresponds to the sensitivity
for the LOW, NORMAL and HIGH SBP state predictions made on the validation set. The
fifth column is the average sensitivity for all three prediction classes.

the HIGH and LOW are combined into one class called ABNORMAL, and tested with an
equal amount of NORMAL observations. NORMAL/LOW is where all HIGH observations
are removed from the data to see if the model can distinguish between these two classes.
NORMAL/HIGH is where the LOW observations are removed, and LOW/HIGH is where
the NORMAL observations are removed. These four tests were to discover what kind of
data the model was good at recognizing and distinguishing from other data.
so the HIGH and LOW class gets larger. Approximately 1/3 of the observations will
be classified as LOW, 1/3 will be NORMAL, and 1/3 will be HIGH.
• SBP State 2 class. Here the data balanced in two classes is used, and the random
forest has to predict if the data is NORMAL or ABNORMAL.
• SBP State only NORMAL and LOW. To make the number of states the model has
to predict smaller, a model is trained only on NORMAL and LOW data, to see if the
model is better to predict when there only are two response classes.
• SBP State only NORMAL and HIGH. In this test, the focus is to see if the model can
distinguish between NORMAL and HIGH SBP states.
• SBP State only LOW and HIGH. In this test, the focus is to see if the model can
distinguish between LOW and HIGH SBP states.
5.4
Select Features
In this section, the features for the ML model have been tested to find the appropriate
features giving the best results. First the number of accepted NaN values in the time
frame before a SBP measurement has been tested. Next, it was tested how many minutes
of data were needed to make the prediction, followed by optimization of the number of
calibration values. Both PCA, ANOVA, and MRMR tests have been used to select the
number of features.
• Accepted NaN: 0, 5, 10, 20, 25
• Time before SBP: 21, 27, 28, 29, 30, 35, 40,
• Number of calibration values: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7
• Data type: olorgreenVital signs and segments with calibration values
• PCA explained variance: 90, 95, 99, 99.9, 99.99, 99.999, All features
• ANOVA: 50, 100, 140, 145, All features
• MRMR: 25, 50, 100, 140, All features
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs

==PAGE:77==
5.8
Post processing
Figure 5.8 shows results from the same model as seen results from in figure 5.7 after
it was post processed. The aim of the post processing was to decrease the number of
false positive observations. For the NORMAL observations that were predicted as HIGH
observations, the post processing decreased the number from 268 to 204 on the test
set. For the true NORMAL observations that were predicted as LOW, the post processing
decreased the number from 341 to 274 on the test set. The number of LOW that was
predicted as HIGH has dropped from 10 to 6 on the test set. The number of HIGH and
LOW that was predicted as NORMAL has slightly increased after post processing, which
also was expected.
Figure 5.8: combined model plus post processing
Figure 5.8 shows the combined model where the post processing algorithm described
in section 4.4.5. Compared with the result from figure 5.7 it can be seen that the post
processing algorithm has increased the overall accuracy of the model by 8.6% for the
validation set and by 5.9 % for the test set.
5.9
Test of final model
The test of the final model can be found in figure 5.8. The final model has an overall
accuracy of 74.5% on the test set and 69.5 % on the validation set. The final model has
a low sensitivity of HIGH values at 14 %. The sensitivity is at 52 % for the LOW values
and 76 % for the NORMAL values.

MIMIC Vital Signs Results

5.10.1
Summary for test with MIMIC
Figure 5.9 shows results from the best performing model that was created using the MIMIC
data. Despite the large amount of data in the MIMIC database, none of the models ended
up performing good enough to be used in the final model. This project focuses on testing
if vital signs can be used to classify the SBP, which means having the vital signs and the
SBP recorded at the same time is crucial. Since the patients recorded in the MIMIC data is
not wearing all the measuring devices at the same time, it turned out that when balancing
the data in the 3­class distribution, it was only possible to make a data set of 180 SBP
measurements. It was decided not to continue with the MIMIC data, because of the bad
results.
70
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:79==
6
Discussion
6.1
Performance of model
The performance of the final model leaves room for improvement, because the model
predicts a lot of false positive NORMAL values. These false positive NORMAL SBP’s are
a problem because they would course an alarm that would make the nurses or doctors
check the patient, even though the patient is in the NORMAL SBP state. It is estimated
that it takes around five minutes to check all the vital signs [56]. The 204+274=478 wrong
classified false positive NORMAL values distributed on the 98 patients in the test set would
waste 24 minutes per patient based on the test set.
(204 + 274) · 5 min
98 patients
= 24 minutes per patient
(6.1)
The 12+24=36 false negative HIGH and LOW classifications from the test patients could
be very dangerous to the patients and it would correspond to almost every third of all the
test patients would miss an alarm that should have been raised. Regarding the LOW ob­
servations that were classified as HIGH, or HIGH that were classified as LOW, it can also
lead to a dangerous situation, even though an alarm is raised. The alarm will be raised
on false basis, which can result in deprioritization from the doctors, if they for instance
deprioritize hypotension. In the final model there are 6 observations that were classified
as HIGH, but in fact they were LOW.
6.2
Data
6.2.1
Number of HIGH and LOW SBP
To train a good performing ML model it requires a lot of data so the model learns how
the data in the areas of interest looks. Recent surveys have shown that around 80 % of
successful ML projects use training sets with more than 1 million useful observations [57].
In this project, the training set is below 1000 observations. If the ML model developed
in this project should be successful it would require much more data, especially in the
LOW and HIGH SBP range. The problem could be solved if the inter arterial SBP was
monitored along with the other vital signs and segments, since arterial SBP could have a
higher sampling frequency. Having a SBP measurement every minute would increase the
SBP measurements in the HIGH and LOW SBP area significantly, and thereby make more
data for training purposes. From table 4.1 it can be seen that nearly 50% of the HIGH
SBP observations and 69% of the LOW SBP observation are lost during processing of
the data. This is because the ECG and PPG data has not been sampled at the same time
as the SBP.
6.2.2
Time asynchronous measurements
The data collected from the WARD devices are not synchronized in time when they are
sent to the beside gateway device, because of a delay in the data transfer process. If
the data was time synchronized a simple PTT approach could have been used. A wired
connection between the ECG device, the PPG device, the BP device, and the gateway
device could solve this problem but it would be inconvenient to have wires connected to
the patient that would make it more difficult for the patient to move around.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
71
==PAGE:80==
6.3
Over­fitting
Over­fitting is when the model gets very good at predicting data it already knows, but
when it has to make predictions based on unseen data, the performance drops signifi­
cant. Over­fitting can be caused by many factors, some of them also seen in this project.
During interpolation, the new values generated will be placed on a straight line between
two original points. At the 5­fold cross validation the performance of the model became
extremely good at the predictions, but when the model was introduced to the uninterpo­
lated validation set, the performance dropped to an unacceptable level. This is because
the interpolated data makes patterns which the model use to make its predictions. Over­
fitting was also seen when optimizing the number of trees. If too many trees were used,
the performance of the model began to decrease slowly.
Another way over­fitting can be introduced, is if the random forest is trained with too many
splits. This is because the model becomes excessive complex and learns the training data
too well, which results in a model that cannot predict on any data sets different from the
training set.
6.4
Regulatory
Since there is no standard for making a classification model to predict SBP it is difficult
to know what the accuracy of the model must be to state that it is in compliance with the
standards. However, the standard can still be used to select or collect new data because
it states number of patients, age distribution, gender and most importantly the distribution
of the SBP values that should be compared with invasive SBP measurements.
6.5
Compare with other studies
The idea of predicting SBP based on vital signs or raw signals from the body is not new,
and in section 3 many projects and results were presented. Two of the projects that
ended up in a model that complies with the standard are based on pulse transit time. This
approach requires synchronization in the time between the two devices, which is not the
case in the WARD project making a direct comparison impossible.
The project with the best performance is ”Yuriy Kurylyak, Francesco Lamonaca, and
Domenico Grimaldi, 2013 A Neural Network based method for continuous blood pressure
estimation from a PPG signal” explained in section 3.0.1. In this project, all 60 seconds of
raw data from each minute were used and the model is a regression model so it would be
impossible to compare the performance of the model with the model made in this project.
But the model seems a good foundation for future work, it would be interesting to try to
use the same approach and modify it to only use 10 seconds from every minute and down
sample the signal to 75 HZ to make it possible for the model to be used on the WARD
PPG segment data.
6.6
Future work
6.6.1
Add personal features
As described in section 2.4.2, many factors seems to affect SBP. Having information about
sex, age, and BMI, which are highly correlated with SBP, could be useful features for the
ML model. Furthermore, information about if the patient is resting, walking, or lying in
bed would also be useful, since all vital signs will be affected by the activity of the patient.
Finally, many types of medications are known for affecting different systems, such as the
heart, respiration, or the vascular system. If the patients are receiving medications, it will
just be another unknown factor for the ML model. If all these extra features could be used
as input for the ML model, it would most likely affect the results in a positive direction.
72
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:81==
Unfortunately, these features are being used in an ongoing Ph.D. project in the WARD
research group, and therefore it was not possible to access these features in this project.
6.6.2
Collect more data
The amount of available data after balancing was very small. The problem is the low
amount of data from when the patients are in the critical SBP states. If a ML model
should be able to detect the SBP states, more data in the critical areas is required. Using
the cuff based BP monitor is a time consuming method, so more data could be collected
using inter arterial SBP to increase the sampling frequency of the SBP. Since all other vital
signs and segments are recorded once every minute, also one SBP every minute when
the patients are having either HIGH or lOW SBP could be recorded.
6.7
Having data from different data sets
As described in section 4.1 the data used in the final model comes from four different
WARD data sets. One data set with post observational surgery patients, one with obser­
vational COPD patients, one with high risk patients from a randomized clinical trial and
one with patients undergoing a major surgery. Since the patients are in different condi­
tions, it is most likely that there is a difference in for instance what a patient with a HIGH
SBP state in the different data sets would look like, because the patients have different
diseases and are on different medication and comes from different age distributions. The
good thing about having patients with different conditions and from different hospitals is
that it makes the model more generalized that would work for more types of patients, and
not only for a specific type.
6.7.1
Delay coursed by calibration and post processing
The 6 calibration values used when calculating the features will cause a delay of 3 hours
where both SBP, ECG and PPG must be measured. This is not very convenient in a
hospital setting where patients are monitored for a short period of time but for patients
that should be monitored for several days the delay is not as big a problem. The post
processing will add a delay of 3 ECG and PPG measurements corresponding to 3 minutes
in the beginning of the monitoring and a delay of 1 ECG and PPG measurement during the
following monitoring. This is because both an observation before and after the observation
of interest is needed.
6.7.2
Personal model
A way to improve the model would be to make personal models for every patient like it
was done in Ying Gu et. al. 2022 ­ ”Personalized Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation
Based on Continuous Vital Signs for PostOperative Patients” described in section 3.0.4.
If a model was trained on each patient each model does not have to be able to account
for the variation from patient to patient. The problem with making personal models is that
it requires a lot of training data from each patient which would cause a large delay in the
measurements. In Ying Gu’s project, this delay is one day, since the personal model is
trained on data from one day.
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
73
==PAGE:82==
7
Conclusion
A model with a validation accuracy of 69.5 % and a test accuracy of 74.5% was made
in this project. This accuracy is at the moment not good enough to be used in a clinical
setting, but the results looks promising for future work. For future work more data is
required, especially in the HIGH and LOW end, but generally it is a challenge that the
data is not recorded at the same time, which results in a lot of useless data. For further
data collection it is crucial that the devices are connected at the same period of time.
In section 5.7 it is presented that two random forest models using 100 trees, and maximum
of 2000 splits, where one is trained on vital sign features and one is trained on segment
features, in a combination is the best model to solve the classification problem.
It was found that the features calculated from the vital sign data was good at predicting
LOW SBP and features calculated from the segment data was good at predicting HIGH
HIGH SBP.
The work with the available data is a solid foundation for future research within the field
of BP estimation, based on vital signs and segments.
74
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs

A.0.2
Features
The 150 features used in this project can be seen in figure A.3, A.4, and A.5. Figure A.1
explains the number in the end of all the ECG segments features. Figure A.2 explains
the number in the end of all the PPG segments features. Feature 1­54 are the vital signs
features used in the model that predicts the LOW SBP. Feature 55­150 are the segments
features used in the model that predicts the HIGH SBP. All features named ”cali” are the
calibration features calculated from the first 6 observations.

function [patient] = Read_data2(folder)
2

3
% data from one patient in each cell

% Date: 13/02/2023
5
% Martin Riedel (s184032) and Benjamin Trasborg (s184038)
6
7

8

9
10
% Initialize an empty cell array to store the file names
11
file_names = cell(length(files), 1);
12
13
% Loop through each file and store its name in the array
14
for i = 1:length(files)
15
[~, name, ext] = fileparts(files(i).name);
16
file_names{i} = [name, ext];
17
file_names{i} = strrep(file_names{i}, 'ECG_segments', '')
18
end
19
20
% make cell array to store data in
21
patient = cell(length(file_names),1);
22
23
% make a loading bar
24
h=waitbar(0,"processing");
25
26
% load data from all patients
27
for i=1:length(file_names)
28
waitbar(i/length(file_names),h);
29
% combine path and file name
30
path=strcat(folder ,file_names{i},'/vital_signs.csv');
31
% test if the file exsist
32
if ~exist(path)
33
warning('Files do not exist for patient %d', i)
34
continue
35
end
36
% load data
37
data=readtable(path);
38
% make column
with patient ID
39
ECG_patientID = repmat(file_names{i},height(data), 1);
40
% combine data and ID
41
t = [table(ECG_patientID) data];
42
patient{i}=t;
43
end
44
close(h);
45
end

Remove Outliers
This function is used to remove outliers from the data. The output format will be a cell
array, and is used by the ”Ward_combined_model_MAIN.m”
1
function [patients1] = removeOutliers2(patients)
2
%This function takes all the patients as input. Outliers are then removed
3
%using the thresholds defined below.
4
5
%Authors: Martin Riedel s184032 and Benjamin Trasborg (s184038)
6
% Date: 10.2.2023
7
8
%Define thresholds for when an observation is considered as an outlier
9
SBP_upper=300;SBP_lower=0;PR_upper=400;
10
PR_lower=0;HR_upper=400;HR_lower=0;RR_upper=160;
11
RR_lower=0;O2_upper=100;O2_lower=20;
12
h=waitbar(0,"processing");
13
14
%Loop for removing outliers in the SBP Data
15
for ii=1:length(patients)
16
waitbar(ii/length(patients),h);
17
for jj=1:length(patients{1,ii}.SystolicBloodPressure)
18
if patients{1,ii}.SystolicBloodPressure(jj) >SBP_upper || ...
19
patients{1,ii}.SystolicBloodPressure(jj) <SBP_lower
20
patients{1,ii}.SystolicBloodPressure(jj)=NaN;
21
end
22
23
if patients{1,ii}.PulseRate(jj) >PR_upper || ...
24
patients{1,ii}.PulseRate(jj) <PR_lower
25
patients{1,ii}.PulseRate(jj)=NaN;
26
end
27
28
if patients{1,ii}.HeartRate(jj) >HR_upper || ...
29
patients{1,ii}.HeartRate(jj) <HR_lower
30
patients{1,ii}.HeartRate(jj)=NaN;
31
end
32
33
if patients{1,ii}.RespirationRate(jj) >RR_upper || ...
34
patients{1,ii}.RespirationRate(jj) <RR_lower
35
patients{1,ii}.RespirationRate(jj)=NaN;
36
end
37
38
if patients{1,ii}.SpO2(jj) >O2_upper || ...
39
patients{1,ii}.SpO2(jj) <O2_lower
40
patients{1,ii}.SpO2(jj)=NaN;
41
end
42
end
43
%Patients1 is the new tabel containing the filtered data
44
patients1=patients;
45
end
46
close(h);

Find SBP state
This function is used to calculate the SBP state for each SBP measurement. The output

SBP state according to table 1.1
1
function [p2]=BPState4(patients)
2
% This function caluculates the SBP State
3
% Input:
4
%
patients=cell array with patient data
5
% Output
6
%
p2 = cell array with patient data plus new collumn with SBP state
7
%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
8
%Authors: Martin Riedel s184032 and Benjamin Trasborg (s184038)
9
% date: 02-03-2023

%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
11

12

nightEnd = datetime('10:00:00', 'InputFormat', 'HH:mm:ss');
14

15
nightEnd = timeofday(nightEnd);
16
17

18
% Loop through all the patients
19

20

21

24

27

28

30

significant") & isbetween(timeofday(p.Timestamp), nightEnd , nightStart);
31
lowNight = round(p.SystolicBloodPressure ,5, "significant") < round(91.00 ,5,
"significant") & ~isbetween(timeofday(p.Timestamp), nightEnd , nightStart);
32
highDay = round(p.SystolicBloodPressure ,5, "significant") >= round(180.00 ,5,
"significant") & isbetween(timeofday(p.Timestamp), nightEnd , nightStart);
33
highNight = round(p.SystolicBloodPressure ,5, "significant") >= round(180.00
,5, "significant") & ~isbetween(timeofday(p.Timestamp), nightEnd ,
nightStart);
34
35
% make all NORMAL
36
BPSValues(:,:)='NORMAL';
37
% make all NaN SBP empty
38
BPSValues(isnan(p.SystolicBloodPressure))='';
39

41
% time point where the BP is low
42

43
% where the BP is low shiftet one place
44
times1_lowDay=['1800-01-01 00:00:00';times_lowDay(1:end-1)];
45
% find indexes where the time diffrence is less than 30 minutes
46
if ~isempty(times_lowDay(minutes((times_lowDay -times1_lowDay))<31))

times1_lowDay))<31),'row');
48
% set the BP state to LOW
49
%new_idx_lowDay=idx_lowDay(p.SystolicBloodPressure((idx_lowDay -1))<91);
50
BPSValues(idx_lowDay)="LOW";
51
end
52
53
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% LOW Night (2*BP < 91)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
54
% time point where the BP is low
55
times_lowNight=datetime(p.Timestamp(lowNight));
56
% where the BP is low shiftet one place
57
times1_lowNight=['1800-01-01 00:00:00';times_lowNight(1:end-1)];
58
59
if ~isempty(times_lowNight(minutes((times_lowNight -times1_lowNight))<61))
60
% find indexes where the time diffrence is less than 60 minutes
61
idx_lowNight=ismember(p.Timestamp ,times_lowNight(minutes((times_lowNight -
times1_lowNight))<61),'row');
62
% set the BP state to LOW
63
BPSValues(idx_lowNight)="LOW";
64
end
65
66
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% High Day (2*BP > 180)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
67
% time point where the BP is low
68
times_highDay=datetime(p.Timestamp(highDay));
69
% where the BP is high shiftet one place
70
times1_highDay=['1800-01-01 00:00:00';times_highDay(1:end-1)];
71
% find indexes where the time diffrence is less than 30 minutes
72
if ~isempty(times_highDay(minutes((times_highDay -times1_highDay))<31))
73
idx_highDay=ismember(p.Timestamp ,times_highDay(minutes((times_highDay -
times1_highDay))<31),'row');
74
% set the BP state to high
75
BPSValues(idx_highDay)="HIGH";
76
end
77
78
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% High Night (2*BP < 91)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
79
% time point where the BP is high
80
times_highNight=datetime(p.Timestamp(highNight));
81
% where the BP is high shiftet one place
82
times1_highNight=['1800-01-01 00:00:00';times_highNight(1:end-1)];
83
if ~isempty(times_highNight(minutes((times_highNight -times1_highNight))<61))
84
% find indexes where the time diffrence is less than 30 minutes
85
idx_highNight=ismember(p.Timestamp ,times_highNight(minutes((times_highNight -
times1_highNight))<61),'row');
86
% set the BP state to high
87
BPSValues(idx_highNight)="HIGH";
88
end
89
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% LOW (BP < 70)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
90
BPSValues(p.SystolicBloodPressure <70)="LOW";
91
92
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% HIGH (BP > 220)
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
93
BPSValues(p.SystolicBloodPressure >220)="HIGH";
94
% add results to a cell array
95
p2{i} = addvars(p, BPSValues , 'NewVariableNames', 'BPS_State1');
96
end
97
close(h);
98
end

Interpolation
Matlab function to make linear interpolation of data, and is used by the ”Ward_combined_model_MAIN.m”

function [patients_interpoleated] = Interpolation4(patients_filtered ,Column)
2
% This Function takes the cell array with all the patient data as input and
outputs a cell
3
% array where the data has been linear interpolated
4
% Author: Benjamin Trasborg s184038 and Martin (s184032)
5
% Date: 17-02-2023
6
% make process bar
7
h=waitbar(0,"processing");
8
% loop through all the patients
9
for patient_i=1:length(patients_filtered)
10
waitbar(patient_i/length(patients_filtered),h);
11
%define patient i as p
12
p=patients_filtered{1,patient_i};
13
%define p as the first 6 features from patient_filtered
14
p=p(:,1:7);
15
% make a variable containing the timestamp from patient i
16
time1=p(:,2).Timestamp;
17
% make a variable containing the SBP values from patient i
18
SBP=p{:,Column};
19
% find the index og all non Nan SBP values
20
idxSBP=find(~isnan(SBP));
21
% enter loop if there are more than 2 SBP measurements for the patient
22
% i
23
if length(idxSBP)>2
24
% make a variable that contains the time in minutes between two SBP
25
% measurements
26
timeBetween2data=idxSBP(2:end)-idxSBP(1:end-1);
27
% make an array with all the indexes that where there are more than
28
% 61 minutes between two SBP measuremtns
29
timetooLong=find(timeBetween2data >61);
30
% make linear interpolation on all the SBP values that are less
31
% then 61 minutes apart
32
vq = interp1(time1(idxSBP),SBP(idxSBP),time1,'linear');
33
time1idx=(1:length(time1));
34
SBP(time1idx)=vq;
35
% check if there are any SBP measuremtens that are more than 61 min
36
% apart
37
if ~isempty(timetooLong)
38
% loop through all the measuremtens that are more than 61 min
39
% apart from 2 to end-1
40
for i = 1:length(timetooLong)
41
SBP(idxSBP(timetooLong(i)):idxSBP(timetooLong(i)+1))=nan;
42
end
43
end
44
patients_filtered{1,patient_i}.(Column)=SBP;
45
%patients_filtered{1,patient_i}.Timestamp=patients_filtered{1,
patient_i}.Timestamp+minutes(2);
46
end
47
end
48
patients_interpoleated=patients_filtered;
49
close(h)
50
end

function [segments_combined]=joinSegmentAndBPState(segments_BP_State ,
finistable)
2
% This Function join the segnemt data and the vital sign data based on the
3
% Timestamp and Paitent ID
4
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
5
% Inputs:
6
%
segments_BP_State = Segment data
7
%
finistable
= vital sign data
8
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
9
% Author: Benjamin Trasborg s184038
10
% date 01-05-2023
11
% counter of number of joined rows
12
count=0;
13
% loop through all segment data patients
14
for i = 1:length(segments_BP_State)
15
% skip patient if there are no segment data
16
if isempty(segments_BP_State{i,1})
17
continue
18
end
19
% loop through all vital sign data
20
for ii =1:length(finistable)
21
% skip patient if there are no segment data
22
if isempty(finistable{ii,1})
23
continue
24
end
25
% if Patient ID is the same in Seegmetn data and vital sign data
26
if strcmp(segments_BP_State{i,1}(1,1).ECG_patientID ,finistable{ii
,1}(1,1).ECG_patientID)
27
% increase counter by 1
28
count=count+1;
29
% rename time column in vital sign data
30
renamed_seg=renamevars(finistable{ii,1}, 'Var1', 'Timestamp');
31
% join the data based on patient ID and Timestamp
32
joined_segments=join(segments_BP_State{i,1},renamed_seg , 'Keys', {
'Timestamp','ECG_patientID'});
33
% add data to a cell array
34
segments_combined{count}=joined_segments;
35
end
36
end
37
end
38
end

Matlab/Find_features_Personal_segment_plus_vital2.m
Segment_Features
Matlab function to calculate segment features, and is used by the ”Ward_combined_model_MAIN.m”
1
function [R_peak_amplitude_mean ,R_peak_amplitude_sd ,R_peak_amplitude_min ,

% function to calculate the derivative
225
function [dy,x] = derivative(x,y,Q_or_S)
226
% number of subintervals
227
N = length(x)-1;
228
% preallocates vector to store derivative
229
dy = zeros(size(x));
230
% approximates derivative at lower bound using forward difference
231
dy(1) = (y(2)-y(1))/(x(2)-x(1));
232
% approximates derivative at upper bound using backward difference
233
dy(N+1) = (y(N+1)-y(N))/(x(N+1)-x(N));
234
% approximates derivatives at all other nodes using central
differences
235
if Q_or_S=='S'
236
for i = 2:N
237
dy(i) = (y(i+1)-y(i-0))/(x(i+1)-x(i-0));
138
Cuffless Blood Pressure Estimation Based on Continuous Vital Signs
==PAGE:147==
238
end
239
else
240
for i = 2:N
241
dy(i) = (y(i)-y(i-1))/(x(i)-x(i-1));
242
end
243
end
244
end
245
end

3

4

PKL­files to CSV in Python
The following python code converts the WARD Segment data PKL­files to CSV­files.
1
# import libraries
2
import os
3
import pandas as pd
4
import pickle
5
6
# create an empty DataFrame to store the data
7
df = pd.DataFrame()
8
folder_path2 = f'/Volumes/WININSTALL/PPG_segments/PPG_segments_COPD'
# path
to save CSV-files
9
#folder_path2 = f'/Volumes/WININSTALL/PPG_segments/ECG_segments_COPD'
# path
to save CSV-files
10
# loop through the folders
11
for i in range(0, 203):
12
13
folder_path = f'/Volumes/Department/Sund/Public/WARD/Datasets/
Observational_COPD/{i}'
# path to where PPG data is stored
14
file_path = os.path.join(folder_path , 'PPG_segments.pkl')
# joind path
and file name to file to load
15
#file_path = os.path.join(folder_path , 'ECG_segments.pkl')
# joind path
and file name to file to load
16
file_path2 = os.path.join(folder_path2 , f'PPG_segments{i}')
# joind path
and file name to file to save
17
#file_path2 = os.path.join(folder_path2 , f'ECG_segments{i}')
# joind path
and file name to file to save
18
try:
19
if os.path.isfile(file_path):
20
# read the file
21
with open(file_path , 'rb') as f:

data = pickle.load(f)
23
# assuming your DataFrame is called df
24
column_names = df.columns.tolist()
25
# save the DataFrame to a CSV file
26
data.to_csv(file_path2)

print(f"{file_path} done")

else:
29
print(f"{file_path} does not exist.")
30
except:
31
print('Could not convert file')
